## Section 2.2 Constructions

In your study of constructions for the Junior Cycle, you will have learned:

- How to bisect a line segment
- How to bisect an angle
- How to construct various triangles
- How to draw parallel and perpendicular lines.

In this section we will deal with seven new constructions that are on the Leaving Certificate course as well as the application of these constructions to real-life situations. For these constructions you will need a compass, straight edge and protractor.

When you use a compass you must leave the construction arcs as evidence that you have used the correct method.

## 1. Constructing an angle of $60^{\circ}$

Each angle in an equilateral triangle is $60^{\circ}$. We will now use this information to draw an angle of $60^{\circ}$.

In an equilateral triangle, all the sides are equal in length.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-055.jpg?height=612&width=1402&top_left_y=406&top_left_x=209)
2. How to construct a tangent to a circle at a given point on it
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-055.jpg?height=291&width=289&top_left_y=1255&top_left_x=254)

Given a circle k and a point $X$ on it. $O$ is the centre of the circle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-055.jpg?height=380&width=487&top_left_y=1201&top_left_x=577)

Join X to O , the centre of the circle. Place a ruler along OX and slide set square along the ruler until it reaches $X$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-055.jpg?height=390&width=517&top_left_y=1201&top_left_x=1076)

Draw a line $t$ through X perpendicular to OX . $t$ is a tangent to the circle k .
3. How to construct a parallelogram, given the lengths of the sides and the measures of the angles
The instructions below show how to construct a parallelogram ABCD where $|\mathrm{AB}|=3.5 \mathrm{~cm}$, $|\mathrm{AD}|=4 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $|\angle \mathrm{DAB}|=55^{\circ}$.

We first draw a rough sketch of $A B C D$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=367&width=586&top_left_y=175&top_left_x=862)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=343&width=305&top_left_y=625&top_left_x=253)

Draw a horizontal line $[\mathrm{AB}]=3.5 \mathrm{~cm}$. Use a protractor to measure an angle of $55^{\circ}$ at A . Draw a line through A and measure $|\mathrm{AD}|=4 \mathrm{~cm}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=392&width=494&top_left_y=617&top_left_x=625)

Place set square along the line AB . Use a ruler to slide the set square up to the point D . Draw a line through D parallel to AB .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=294&width=437&top_left_y=664&top_left_x=1163)

Use a compass with a radius of 3.5 cm (the same as $|\mathrm{AB}|$ ) to draw an arc on the line. $|\mathrm{DC}|=$ 3.5 cm . Join BC . ABCD is the required parallelogram.

## Circles and triangles

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=312&width=312&top_left_y=1428&top_left_x=400)

The circumcircle of a triangle is the circle which passes through the three vertices, as shown.
The centre, O , of this circle is called the circumcentre of the triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-056.jpg?height=273&width=416&top_left_y=1450&top_left_x=1060)

A circle inscribed in a triangle such that all three sides touch the circle is called the incircle of the triangle. The centre of the incircle is called the incentre of the triangle. In the figure above, O is the incentre.

The construction of the circumcircle and incircle of a triangle will involve two constructions that you studied for your Junior Certificate examination.

The diagrams shown below will help you recall the steps involved in doing these constructions.

## The perpendicular bisector of a line segment The bisector of an angle

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=397&width=1151&top_left_y=231&top_left_x=449)

You should practise these constructions before attempting to draw the circumcircle and incircle of a triangle.

## 4. How to construct the circumcircle of a given triangle

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=289&width=399&top_left_y=841&top_left_x=239)

Construct the perpendicular bisector of [XY].
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=289&width=453&top_left_y=841&top_left_x=700)

Construct the perpendicular bisector of [XZ]. The two bisectors meet at the point O , as shown. O is the circumcentre.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=350&width=345&top_left_y=841&top_left_x=1235)

With O as centre and $|\mathrm{OX}|$ as radius, draw a circle through $\mathrm{X}, \mathrm{Y}$ and Z . This is the circumcircle of the triangle.

## 5. How to construct the incircle of a given triangle

The construction of the incircle of a triangle involves constructing the bisector of an angle which is given on the previous page.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=291&width=343&top_left_y=1665&top_left_x=262)

Construct the bisector of $\angle \mathrm{XYZ}$, as shown.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=291&width=329&top_left_y=1665&top_left_x=689)

Now construct the bisector of $\angle \mathrm{XZY}$. The two bisectors meet at the point I. I is the incentre.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-057.jpg?height=305&width=328&top_left_y=1663&top_left_x=1175)

Use a set square to draw a perpendicular from I to the line YZ . The perpendicular meets YZ at H . With $|\mathrm{IH}|$ as radius, draw a circle to touch the three sides. This is the incircle of the triangle XYZ.

## 6. How to construct the centroid of a triangle

The line segment joining the vertex of a triangle to the midpoint of the opposite side is called a median.

In the triangle below, $[\mathrm{XM}]$ is a median.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=261&width=435&top_left_y=414&top_left_x=294)

The point of intersection of the three medians of a triangle is called the centroid of the triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=244&width=328&top_left_y=437&top_left_x=951)

## G is the centroid.

To find the midpoint of any line segment, we construct the perpendicular bisector of that line segment.

The diagram on the right should help you recall the steps involved in this construction.

The three diagrams below illustrate the steps to be followed in the construction of the centroid of a triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=402&width=492&top_left_y=657&top_left_x=1140)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=282&width=366&top_left_y=1099&top_left_x=253)

Construct the perpendicular bisector of [XZ], as shown. M is the midpoint of [XZ].
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=289&width=371&top_left_y=1093&top_left_x=710)

Now construct the perpendicular bisector of $[\mathrm{XY}]$. N is the midpoint of [XY].
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=286&width=369&top_left_y=1097&top_left_x=1192)

Join YM and ZN. They meet at the point $\mathrm{G} . \mathrm{G}$ is the centroid of the triangle.

## 7. How to construct the orthocentre of a triangle

The perpendicular line segment drawn from the vertex of a triangle to the opposite side is called an altitude.

The point of intersection of the three altitudes of a triangle is called the orthocentre.

The point H is called the orthocentre.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-058.jpg?height=489&width=681&top_left_y=1598&top_left_x=952)

The diagrams below illustrate the steps involved in the construction of the orthocentre of a triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=390&width=411&top_left_y=291&top_left_x=252)

Place the ruler along XZ and use the set square to draw the line YA perpendicular to XZ.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=381&width=444&top_left_y=293&top_left_x=709)

Use the ruler and set square to draw the line XB perpendicular to YZ .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=303&width=395&top_left_y=351&top_left_x=1203)

The lines YA and XB meet at the point $\mathrm{H} . \mathrm{H}$ is the orthocentre of the triangle.

## Applications of the given constructions

In the given diagram, the line $\ell$ is the perpendicular bisector of $[\mathrm{AB}]$.
Any point on this bisector is the same distance from A and B.
Thus $|\mathrm{AX}|=|\mathrm{XB}|$.

OP is the bisector of $\angle \mathrm{AOB}$. Any point on this bisector is equidistant from the arms of the angle. $|\mathrm{XY}|=|\mathrm{XZ}|$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=499&width=970&top_left_y=941&top_left_x=662)

Take any three points $\mathrm{X}, \mathrm{Y}$ and Z .
How do we find a point that is the same distance from all three points?
Construct the perpendicular bisectors of $[\mathrm{XY}]$ and $[\mathrm{YZ}]$.
Name these lines $\ell$ and $m$.
Any point on $\ell$ is equidistant from X and Y .
Any point on $m$ is equidistant from Y and Z .
The lines $\ell$ and $m$ intersect at K .
K is equidistant from $\mathrm{X}, \mathrm{Y}$ and Z .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=399&width=553&top_left_y=1465&top_left_x=1079)

Equidistant means 'the same distance'.
The largest circle that can be drawn inside a triangle is the incircle, that is, the circle that touches all three sides.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-059.jpg?height=268&width=435&top_left_y=1917&top_left_x=928)

## Exercise 2.2

1. (i) The diagram on the right shows a rough sketch of the parallelogram ABCD .
Construct this parallelogram if its perpendicular height is 4 cm .
(ii) Draw the diagonals $[\mathrm{AC}]$ and $[\mathrm{BD}]$. Does your construction verify that the diagonals bisect each other?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-060.jpg?height=287&width=501&top_left_y=243&top_left_x=1131)
(iii) Now prove that the diagonal [AC] bisects the diagonal [BD].
2. Draw the parallelogram ABCD in which the base $[\mathrm{AB}]=4.5 \mathrm{~cm},|\mathrm{BC}|=3 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $|\mathrm{AC}|=6 \mathrm{~cm}$. Measure $\angle \mathrm{ABC}$.
3. Draw a triangle of sides $6 \mathrm{~cm}, 5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and 4 cm . Now construct the circumcircle of this triangle. Show all construction lines.
4. Draw the right-angled triangle, as shown on the right. Now construct the circumcircle of this triangle. What do you notice about the circumcentre of the triangle?

Now draw any other right-angled triangle and construct the circumcircle.

Did you get the same result as you got for the first triangle? What conclusion is suggested regarding the circumcentre of a right-angled triangle?
5. The diagram on the right shows a rough sketch of a triangle with sides $7 \mathrm{~cm}, 4.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and 3.5 cm .
Make an accurate drawing of this triangle and construct the circumcircle.

You should find that the centre of the circumcircle is outside the triangle.

What is the connection between the type of triangle you have drawn and the location of the circumcentre?
Use the diagram on the right to explain why the circumcentre of an obtuse-angled triangle will always lie outside the triangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-060.jpg?height=360&width=362&top_left_y=1739&top_left_x=1276)
6. The diagram shows three villages, Drum, Moore and Tubber.

The distances between the villages are shown.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-061.jpg?height=321&width=633&top_left_y=271&top_left_x=603)

Using the scale $1 \mathrm{~cm}=1 \mathrm{~km}$, make an accurate drawing of the diagram above.
It is planned to build a school that is equidistant from the three villages.
Show on your drawing where the school should be built.
7. Using a compass and ruler only, construct an angle of $60^{\circ}$.
8. Draw a triangle of sides $6.5 \mathrm{~cm}, 5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and 4 cm .

Use the bisectors of any two angles of the triangle to find the centre of its incircle.
Now draw the incircle.
9. ABC is a triangle with sides of length $a, b$ and c , as shown. Its incircle has radius $r$ and centre O .
(i) Express the area of $\triangle \mathrm{BOC}$ in terms of $a$ and $r$.
(ii) Hence show that the area of $\triangle \mathrm{ABC}$ is

$$
\frac{1}{2} r(a+b+c) .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-061.jpg?height=359&width=708&top_left_y=1077&top_left_x=921)
10. The line XD is the bisector of $\angle \mathrm{AXB}$.

K is a point on $\mathrm{XD}, \mathrm{KZ} \perp \mathrm{AX}$ and $\mathrm{KY} \perp \mathrm{XB}$.
Show that the triangles XKZ and XKY are congruent.
Hence show that $|\mathrm{KZ}|=|\mathrm{KY}|$.
What conclusion can you come to regarding any point on the bisector of an angle?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-061.jpg?height=468&width=744&top_left_y=1501&top_left_x=887)
11. In the given diagram, $p$ is a transversal intersecting two parallel lines $\ell$ and $m$.
Draw a rough sketch to show the locations of the points X and Y , each of which is equidistant from the three lines.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-061.jpg?height=267&width=392&top_left_y=1993&top_left_x=1237)
12. A campsite is in the shape of a triangle with busy roads running along all three sides of the site.
The sides of the site are $110 \mathrm{~m}, 150 \mathrm{~m}$ and 170 m in length.
(i) Using $20 \mathrm{~m}=1 \mathrm{~cm}$, draw a scaled diagram of this site.
(ii) Show on the diagram the best position to pitch a tent so that it is as far away as possible from all three roads. Show your construction lines.
13. ABC is a right-angled triangle, as shown.
(i) Write down the radius of the circumcircle without drawing and measuring it.
(ii) Explain why the point B is the orthocentre of $\triangle \mathrm{ABC}$.
14. Draw any obtuse-angled triangle.

Now construct the orthocentre of the triangle.
Is the orthocentre inside or outside the triangle?
Will this result hold for all obtuse-angled triangles? Explain why.
15. Draw a circle of radius 3 cm and mark the centre O . Mark a point X on this circle.

Now use a ruler and set square to draw a tangent to this circle at the point X .
16. The given figure shows a circle and two chords $[\mathrm{AB}]$ and $[\mathrm{CD}]$. What can you say about the perpendicular bisector of $[\mathrm{AB}]$ ? Now describe how these two chords can be used to find the centre of the circle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-062.jpg?height=348&width=347&top_left_y=1269&top_left_x=1288)
17. In Q16 we used two chords to find the centre of a circle. The given diagram shows a circle and two points on the circle. Describe another way of finding the centre of this circle using the points X and Y .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-062.jpg?height=352&width=328&top_left_y=1658&top_left_x=1307)
18. Construct an equilateral triangle of side 5 cm .
(i) Now construct the circumcentre, O , of the triangle.
(ii) Explain why O is also the incentre of the triangle.

## Revision Exercise 2 (Core)

Note: For completeness several revision questions are based on the geometry that you have learnt in book 4. It is therefore advisable to revise Geometry 1 before proceeding with these revision questions.

1. The area of the parallelogram ABCD is $40 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$. If $|\mathrm{DB}|=15 \mathrm{~cm}$, find $|\mathrm{AE}|$, where $\mathrm{AE} \perp \mathrm{DB}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-063.jpg?height=248&width=338&top_left_y=390&top_left_x=1123)
2. Gary, $G$, can just see the top of a radio mast, $R$, over a wall, $W$.

Gary is 15 m from the wall. The wall is 45 m from the radio mast. The wall is 2.7 m high.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-063.jpg?height=298&width=617&top_left_y=813&top_left_x=611)

Calculate the height of the radio mast, marked $h$ on the diagram.
3. In the given triangles, the equal angles are marked.
(i) Explain why the two triangles are similar.
(ii) Find the values of $x$ and $y$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-063.jpg?height=281&width=536&top_left_y=1229&top_left_x=1095)
4. The triangle $A B C$ has been enlarged positively and negatively as shown in diagram.
(i) Find the scale factor used in each case.

Calculate the area of the triangle $A B C$ and hence
(ii) deduce the area of triangles $E F G$ and HIJ using the scale factors found in (i)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-063.jpg?height=628&width=574&top_left_y=1572&top_left_x=1057)
5. In the given circle, O is the centre.
(i) Find the sizes of the angles marked $x$ and $y$.
(ii) Explain why a circle drawn on $[\mathrm{AC}]$ as diameter must pass through O .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-064.jpg?height=364&width=364&top_left_y=174&top_left_x=1195)
6. In the given figure, PQRS is a parallelogram. State whether each of the following is true or false, giving a reason for answer:
(i) $|\angle \mathrm{TQR}|+|\angle \mathrm{QRS}|=180^{\circ}$
(ii) $|\angle \mathrm{PQR}|+|\angle \mathrm{RST}|=180^{\circ}$
(iii) $|\angle \mathrm{QTS}|+|\angle \mathrm{QRS}|=180^{\circ}$
(iv) $|\angle \mathrm{QPS}|+|\angle \mathrm{QRS}|=180^{\circ}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-064.jpg?height=321&width=329&top_left_y=589&top_left_x=1191)
7. (i) Three people $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}$ and C on a shoreline see a boat at sea. The boat is the same distance from all three people.
Describe how you would locate the position of the boat.
(ii) In the triangle XYZ below, the angles XYZ and XZY are bisected.
If $|\angle \mathrm{YXZ}|=70^{\circ}$, find $|\angle \mathrm{YPZ}|$.
(iii) Is P the incentre?

Explain.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-064.jpg?height=590&width=975&top_left_y=939&top_left_x=658)
8. Draw a triangle of sides $5 \mathrm{~cm}, 6 \mathrm{~cm}$ and 7 cm .

Now construct the circumcircle of the triangle, showing all your construction lines.
9. In the given figure, $\mathrm{AB}^{\prime} \mathrm{C}^{\prime}$ is an enlargement of the triangle ABC , where A is the centre of enlargement. If $|\mathrm{AC}|=8,\left|\mathrm{CC}^{\prime}\right|=12$ and $\left|\mathrm{B}^{\prime} \mathrm{C}^{\prime}\right|=15$, find
(i) the scale factor of the enlargement
(ii) $|\mathrm{BC}|$
(iii) the ratio $|\mathrm{AB}|:\left|\mathrm{AB}^{\prime}\right|$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-064.jpg?height=342&width=470&top_left_y=1696&top_left_x=1161)
10. The height of a picture is $\frac{4}{3}$ its width. The picture is to be enlarged so as to have an area of $192 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$. Find the height and width of the enlarged picture.

## Revision Exercise 2 (Advanced)

1. In the given triangles, the arrows indicate that the lines are parallel.
Mark in the equal angles and hence use similar triangles to find the values of $x$ and $y$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-065.jpg?height=376&width=380&top_left_y=241&top_left_x=1248)
2. In the given figure, $l, m$ and $n$ are parallel lines.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-065.jpg?height=385&width=661&top_left_y=713&top_left_x=589)
(i) If $|\mathrm{AB}|=|\mathrm{BC}|=7 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $|\mathrm{CD}|=8 \mathrm{~cm}$, find $|\mathrm{DE}|$.
(ii) If $|\mathrm{AE}|=12 \mathrm{~cm}$, find $|\mathrm{BD}|$.
(iii) Explain why $\triangle A C E$ is an enlargement of $\triangle B C D$ and determine the scale factor and centre of enlargement.
3. In the given triangle $\mathrm{ABC}, \mathrm{I}$ is the incentre and the radius of the incircle is 3 cm .
What is the area of $\triangle \mathrm{BIC}$ ?
Hence find the area of $\triangle \mathrm{ABC}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-065.jpg?height=329&width=541&top_left_y=1351&top_left_x=1059)
4. Shape B is similar to shape A.
(i) What is the scale factor of the enlargement?
(ii) Find $a$ and $b$.
(iii) A square of area $25 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ is enlarged using a scale factor $k$. Find the scale
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-065.jpg?height=239&width=875&top_left_y=1771&top_left_x=753) factor $k$ if the area of the enlarged square in $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$ has the same numerical value as the perimeter of the enlarged square in cm .
5. BC is parallel to DE .
$|\mathbf{A B}|$ is twice as long as $|\mathbf{B D}|$.
$|\mathrm{AD}|=36 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $|\mathrm{AC}|=27 \mathrm{~cm}$.
(i) Work out the length of $[\mathrm{AB}]$.
(ii) Work out the length of [AE].
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-066.jpg?height=435&width=492&top_left_y=174&top_left_x=994)
6. In the given circle, $O$ is the centre and $|A B|=6 \mathrm{~cm}$. The distance from $O$ to $A B$ is 4 cm and the distance from O to CD is 2 cm . Find the length of the chord $[\mathrm{CD}]$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-066.jpg?height=411&width=392&top_left_y=643&top_left_x=1131)
7. $[\mathrm{PT}]$ is a tangent to the semicircle with centre O . AOBP is a straight line.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-066.jpg?height=265&width=835&top_left_y=1126&top_left_x=549)

If $|\angle \mathrm{TAO}|=28^{\circ}$, find the values of $x, y$ and $z$.
8. (i) A ball of radius $r \mathrm{~cm}$ has a surface area of $20 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$.

Find, in terms of $r$, the radius of a ball with a surface area of $500 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$.
(ii) The length of a rectangle is twice the width of the rectangle.
The length of a diagonal of the rectangle is 25 cm .
Work out the area of the rectangle.
Give your answer as an integer.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-066.jpg?height=225&width=397&top_left_y=1575&top_left_x=1235)
9. In the given figure, $\mathrm{AB} \| \mathrm{DE}$.
(i) Show that the triangles ABC and CDE are similar.
(ii) If $|\mathrm{AB}|=7 \mathrm{~cm},|\mathrm{BC}|=6 \mathrm{~cm},|\mathrm{AC}|=4 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $|\mathrm{CE}|=12 \mathrm{~cm}$, find $|\mathrm{CD}|$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-066.jpg?height=314&width=494&top_left_y=1828&top_left_x=1139)

## 3 Integration

## Key words

antidifferentiation integration integral integrand constant of integration exponential function velocity acceleration definite integral indefinite integral average value of a function intervals

## Section 3.1 Antidifferentiation

Consider the equation $y=x^{2}$.
Differentiating, we get $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=2 x$.
$2 x$ is the derivative of the function $y=x^{2}$.
From this we can see that when given the derivative $2 x$, we can write down the equation of the function, i.e. $y=x^{2}$.

The process of finding a function from its derivative is called antidifferentiation.
Now consider the functions shown: $y=x^{2}, y=x^{2}+1$ and $y=x^{2}+2$.
The derivative of each of these functions is $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=2 x$, as represented by the line shown in red.
The expressions $x^{2}, x^{2}+1$ and $x^{2}+2$ are said to be antiderivatives of $2 x$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-070.jpg?height=404&width=491&top_left_y=1524&top_left_x=674)

Starting with $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=2 x$, we have an infinite number of possibilities for $y$, all of the form $y=x^{2}+c$, where $c$ is an unknown constant.
We use the notation $\int 2 x d x=x^{2}+c$ to state that 'the general antiderivative of $2 x$ with respect to $x$ is $x^{2}+c$ ''

From the examples above, we can see that antidifferentiation is the reverse of differentiation.

Antidifferentiation is more commonly called integration.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { If } f^{\prime}(x) \text { is the derivative of } f(x) \text {, then } \int f^{\prime}(x) \mathrm{d} x=f(x)+c \text {. } \\
& \text { If } \frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x} \text { is the derivative of } y \text {, then } \int\left(\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}\right) \mathrm{d} x=y+c \text {. }
\end{aligned}
$$

Now consider the following functions and their derivatives:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& f(x)=x^{2} \Rightarrow f^{\prime}(x)=2 x \\
& f(x)=x^{3} \Rightarrow f^{\prime}(x)=3 x^{2} \\
& f(x)=x^{7} \Rightarrow f^{\prime}(x)=7 x^{6}
\end{aligned}
$$

Reversing this process, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\int 2 x \mathrm{~d} x=x^{2} & \Rightarrow \int x \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{1}{2} x^{2}+c \\
\int 3 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=x^{3} & \Rightarrow \int x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{1}{3} x^{3}+c \\
\int 7 x^{6} \mathrm{~d} x=x^{7} & \Rightarrow \int x^{6} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{1}{7} x^{7}+c
\end{aligned}
$$

From this pattern, we can see that reversing the rule for differentiation produces a rule for integration as follows:

In general, $\int x^{n} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{x^{n+1}}{n+1}+c, n \neq-1$

## In words <br> To integrate a power of $x$, increase the power by 1 and divide by the new power.

Here are some useful results for integrating expressions that follow from the corresponding results for differentiation.

1. $\int a f(x) \mathrm{d} x=a \int f(x) \mathrm{d} x$, that is, any constant factor may be taken outside the integral sign.
2. $\int(f(x)+g(x)) \mathrm{d} x=\int f(x) \mathrm{d} x+\int g(x) \mathrm{d} x \ldots$ integrate each term separately.
3. $\int a \mathrm{~d} x=a x+c$, since $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x}(a x+c)=a$, where $a$ is a constant.

To integrate a function $f(x)$ with respect to $x$, we write

$$
\int f(x) \mathrm{d} x .
$$

We read this as 'the integral of $f(x)$ with respect to $x$ '.
The function to be integrated, i.e. $f(x)$, is called the integrand.

## Example 1

Find
(i) $\int\left(3 x^{2}+4 x+5\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int(2 x-1)^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$.
(i) $\int\left(3 x^{2}+4 x+5\right) \mathrm{d} x=\frac{3 x^{3}}{3}+\frac{4 x^{2}}{2}+5 x+c$

$$
=x^{3}+2 x^{2}+5 x+c \quad \ldots \text { don't forget to include } c
$$

(ii) $\int(2 x-1)^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=\int\left(4 x^{2}-4 x+1\right) \mathrm{d} x=\frac{4 x^{3}}{3}-\frac{4 x^{2}}{2}+x+c$

$$
=\frac{4 x^{3}}{3}-2 x^{2}+x+c
$$

Some expressions are not in the form $a x^{n}$ or are not written as a sum or difference of functions of this form. In these cases, it is necessary to change the integrand so that each term is in the form $a x^{n}$.

## Example 2

Find
(i) $\int \frac{x^{3}-4 x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int\left(x^{3}+\frac{1}{x^{2}}+\sqrt{x}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(iii) $\int \sqrt{x}(x+4) \mathrm{d} x$
(i) $\int \frac{x^{3}-4 x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\int\left(\frac{x^{3}}{x}-\frac{4 x}{x}\right) \mathrm{d} x=\int\left(x^{2}-4\right) \mathrm{d} x=\frac{x^{3}}{3}-4 x+c$
(ii) $\int\left(x^{3}+\frac{1}{x^{2}}+\sqrt{x}\right) \mathrm{d} x=\int\left(x^{3}+x^{-2}+x^{\frac{1}{2}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{x^{4}}{4}+\frac{x^{-1}}{-1}+\frac{x^{\frac{3}{2}}}{\frac{3}{2}}+c \\
& =\frac{x^{4}}{4}-\frac{1}{x}+\frac{2}{3} x^{\frac{3}{2}}+c
\end{aligned}
$$

(iii) $\int \sqrt{x}(x+4) \mathrm{d} x=\int\left(x^{\frac{3}{2}}+4 x^{\frac{1}{2}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$

$$
=\frac{x^{\frac{5}{2}}}{\frac{5}{2}}+\frac{4 x^{\frac{3}{2}}}{\frac{3}{2}}+c=\frac{2}{5} x^{\frac{5}{2}}+\frac{8}{3} x^{\frac{3}{2}}+c
$$

## Finding the constant of integration

Each of the examples above contain an arbitrary constant $c$.
This arbitrary constant is generally called the constant of integration.
This constant of integration can be found if further information about the function is given.
This is illustrated in the following example.

## Example 3

A curve with equation $y=f(x)$ passes through the point $(2,0)$.
If $f^{\prime}(x)=3 x^{2}-\frac{1}{x^{2}}$, find $f(x)$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
f(x)=\int\left(3 x^{2}-\frac{1}{x^{2}}\right) \mathrm{d} x & =\int\left(3 x^{2}-x^{-2}\right) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\frac{3 x^{3}}{3}-\frac{x^{-1}}{-1}=x^{3}+\frac{1}{x}+c \\
f(x)=x^{3}+\frac{1}{x}+c &
\end{aligned}
$$

Since the curve contains $(2,0)$, then $(2,0)$ satisfies the equation $y=f(x)$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow 0=2^{3}+\frac{1}{2}+c \\
& \Rightarrow 0=\frac{17}{2}+c \Rightarrow c=-\frac{17}{2} \\
& \therefore f(x)=x^{3}+\frac{1}{x}-\frac{17}{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

## Exercise 3.1

1. Find each of the following integrals:
(i) $\int x \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int\left(3 x^{2}+4 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(iv) $\int-2 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$
(v) $\int 3 \mathrm{~d} x$
(vi) $\int\left(-x^{2}+3\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(vii) $\int\left(4 x^{3}+6 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(viii) $\int\left(2 x^{2}-3 x-1\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ix) $\int 12 y^{2} \mathrm{~d} y$

(i) $\int x^{-2} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int 2 x^{-3} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int \frac{3}{x^{2}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int-\frac{2}{x^{3}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(v) $\int \sqrt{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(vi) $\int 3 x^{\frac{1}{2}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(vii) $\int \frac{1}{\sqrt{x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(viii) $\int \sqrt[3]{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ix) $\int 4 \pi r^{2} \mathrm{~d} r$

(ii) $\int\left(\frac{4}{x^{2}}-2+x^{3}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(iii) $\int(4 \sqrt{x}-3) \mathrm{d} x$
(iv) $\int\left(\sqrt{x}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{x}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(v) $\int\left(2 \sqrt{x}-\frac{2}{x^{2}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$

(i) $\int(x-3)^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$

(i) $\int \frac{x^{4}-3 x^{3}+4 x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x$

11. Find $y$ in terms of $x$ in each of the following:
(i) $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=x^{2}+2 x$ and $y=2$ when $x=0$
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=3-x^{2}$ and $y=2$ when $x=3$

(ii) the equation of the curve.

## Section 3.2 Integrating exponential and trigonometric functions

In chapter 7, T\&T4 it was found that if
(i) $f(x)=\mathrm{e}^{x}$, then $f^{\prime}(x)=\mathrm{e}^{x} \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\mathrm{e}^{x}+c$
(ii) $f(x)=\mathrm{e}^{a x}$, then $f^{\prime}(x)=a \mathrm{e}^{a x} \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \mathrm{e}^{a x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{1}{a} \mathrm{e}^{a x}+c$
(iii) $f(x)=a^{x}$, then $f^{\prime}(x)=a^{x} \ln a \Rightarrow \quad \int a^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{a^{x}}{\ln a}+c$

## Example 1

Find each of the following:
(i) $\int \mathrm{e}^{3 x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{4 x}+6 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(iii) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{5 x}+2\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(iv) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{x}+\mathrm{e}^{-x}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(i) $\int \mathrm{e}^{3 x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{1}{3} \mathrm{e}^{3 x}+c$
(ii) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{4 x}+6 x\right) \mathrm{d} x=\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{e}^{4 x}+\frac{6 x^{2}}{2}+c$

$$
=\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{e}^{4 x}+3 x^{2}+c
$$

(iii) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{5 x}+2\right) \mathrm{d} x=\frac{1}{5} \mathrm{e}^{5 x}+2 x+c$
(iv) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{x}+\mathrm{e}^{-x}\right) \mathrm{d} x=\mathrm{e}^{x}+\frac{\mathrm{e}^{-x}}{-1}+c$ $=\mathrm{e}^{x}-\frac{1}{\mathrm{e}^{x}}+c$

## Example 2

Given $y=5^{x}$, show that $\int 5^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{5^{x}}{\ln 5}+c$.
We know that if $y=5^{x} \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=5^{x} \ln 5 \quad$ (check p25 formulae and tables)
$\therefore \mathrm{d} y=5^{x} \ln 5 \mathrm{~d} x$
$\therefore \quad y=\int 5^{x} \ln 5 \mathrm{~d} x$
$\therefore \quad y=\ln 5 \int 5^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
$\therefore \quad \int 5^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{y}{\ln 5}+c=\frac{5^{x}}{\ln 5}+c$

## Integrals of the form $\int \sin a x$ and $\int \cos a x$

In our study of differential calculus, we found the derivatives of $\sin x, \sin a x, \cos x$ and $\cos a x$.

We will now use these results to write down the standard integrals of the basic trigonometric functions.
(i) $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x} \sin x=\cos x \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \cos x \mathrm{~d} x=\sin x+c$
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x} \sin a x=a \cos a x \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \cos a x \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{\sin a x}{a}+c$
(iii) $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x} \cos x=-\sin x \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \sin x \mathrm{~d} x=-\cos x+c$
(iv) $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x} \cos a x=-a \sin a x \quad \Rightarrow \quad \int \sin a x \mathrm{~d} x=-\frac{\cos a x}{a}+c$

## Example 3

Find (i) $\int \cos 4 x d x \quad$ (ii) $\int \sin 3 x d x$.
(i) $\int \cos 4 x \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{\sin 4 x}{4}+c=\frac{1}{4} \sin 4 x+c$
(ii) $\int \sin 3 x \mathrm{~d} x=-\frac{\cos 3 x}{3}+c=-\frac{1}{3} \cos 3 x+c$

## Example 4

If $y=\sin 3 x^{2}$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
Hence, find $\int 6 x \cos 3 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$.
$y=\sin 3 x^{2} \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\cos 3 x^{2} \times \frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x}\left(3 x^{2}\right) \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=6 x \cos 3 x^{2}$
Since $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=6 x \cos 3 x^{2} \Rightarrow \int 6 x \cos 3 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=\sin 3 x^{2}+c$

## Example 5

Let $h(x)=x \ln x, x \in R, x>0$.
(i) Find $h^{\prime}(x)$.
(ii) Hence, find $\int \ln x \mathrm{~d} x$.
(i)

$$
\begin{aligned}
h(x) & =x \ln x \\
h^{\prime}(x) & =x \cdot \frac{1}{x}+\ln x \times 1 \quad \ldots 1 \text { st } \times \text { derivative of } 2 \text { nd }+2 \text { nd } \times \text { derivative of } 1 \text { st } \\
h^{\prime}(x) & =1+\ln x
\end{aligned}
$$

(ii) $\quad \int(1+\ln x) \mathrm{d} x=x \ln x \quad \ldots$ from (i) above

But $\int(1+\ln x) \mathrm{d} x=\int 1 \mathrm{~d} x+\int \ln x \mathrm{~d} x$

$$
\begin{array}{rlrl}
\therefore & & \int 1 \mathrm{~d} x+\int \ln x \mathrm{~d} x & =x \ln x \\
\therefore & & \int \ln x \mathrm{~d} x & =x \ln x-\int 1 \mathrm{~d} x \\
& & =x \ln x-x \\
\therefore & & \int \ln x \mathrm{~d} x & =x \ln x-x+c
\end{array}
$$

## Exercise 3.2

1. Find the following integrals:
(i) $\int \mathrm{e}^{2 x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int 3 \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int 2 \mathrm{e}^{4 x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int \mathrm{e}^{-3 x} \mathrm{~d} x$

(i) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{3 x}+4\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int 4 \mathrm{e}^{\frac{1}{2} x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{4 x}+\frac{1}{\mathrm{e}^{4 x}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
3. If $y=\mathrm{e}^{x^{2}}$, write down $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$. Hence, find $\int 2 x \mathrm{e}^{x^{2}} \mathrm{~d} x$.
4. Find each of the following integrals:
(i) $\int \cos 3 x \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int \sin 4 x \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int-\sin 5 x \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int \cos k x \mathrm{~d} x, k \in N$
5. Integrate each of the following:
(i) $\int 3 \cos 6 x d x$
(ii) $\int(\cos 2 x-\sin 5 x) \mathrm{d} x$
(iii) $\int 3 \cos (-9 x) \mathrm{d} x$
6. Find $\int 3\left(\mathrm{e}^{x}-4 \sin 3 x+2\right) \mathrm{d} x$.
7. Find the following integrals:
(i) $\int\left(4 \mathrm{e}^{2 x}+4 \sin 3 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int(3 \cos x-2 \cos 4 x) d x$
8. Given $y=\cos 4 x^{2}$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.

Hence, find $\int-8 x \sin 4 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$.
9. Given that $\frac{x+y}{z}=\frac{x}{z}+\frac{y}{z}$, use this identity to integrate each of the following:
(i) $\int \frac{\mathrm{e}^{2 x}+4}{\mathrm{e}^{x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int \frac{\mathrm{e}^{x+2}+3}{\mathrm{e}^{x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int \frac{1+3 \mathrm{e}^{x}}{\mathrm{e}^{2 x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
10. Find (i) $\int\left(\mathrm{e}^{x}-\mathrm{e}^{-x}\right)^{2} \mathrm{~d} x \quad$ (ii) $\int\left(3+\mathrm{e}^{x}\right)\left(2+\mathrm{e}^{-x}\right) \mathrm{d} x$.
11. Given $y=7^{x}$, use the rules of logs to find $x$ in terms of $y$.
(i) Hence, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} x}{\mathrm{~d} y}$.
(ii) Now find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ in terms of $x$.
(iii) Use your result from (ii) to show that $\int 7^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{7^{x}}{\ln 7}+c$.
12. Given $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=a \mathrm{e}^{-x}+2, a \in R$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=5$ when $x=0$.

Find an expression for $y$, given that $y=-3$ when $x=0$.
13. The curve for which $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\mathrm{e}^{k x}$, where $k$ is a constant, is such that the tangent at $\left(1, e^{2}\right)$ passes through the origin. Find the gradient of this tangent and hence determine:
(i) the value of $k$
(ii) the equation of the curve.
14. Let $f(x)=2 x \mathrm{e}^{x}$.
(i) Find $f^{\prime}(x)$.
(ii) Hence, find $\int 2 x \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$.
15. Given $f(x)=x \sin x$, find $f^{\prime}(x)$.

Hence, find $\int x \cos x \mathrm{~d} x$.
16. Let $f(x)=4 x \mathrm{e}^{2 x}$.
(i) Find $f^{\prime}(x)$.
(ii) Hence, find $\int 8 x \mathrm{e}^{2 x} \mathrm{~d} x$.
17. Given $y=2 x \mathrm{e}^{3 x}+\cos x$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$. Hence, find $\int 6 x \mathrm{e}^{3 x} \mathrm{~d} x$.

## Section 3.3 Applications of integration

In our study of differential calculus, we learned that in an equation of the type

$$
s=2 t^{2}-3 t+4,
$$

$s$ represents the displacement of a body from some fixed origin after $t$ seconds.
We also learned that $\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ represents the speed or velocity $v$.
The acceleration, $a$, of a body is defined as the rate of change of the velocity with respect to time,
that is, $\frac{\mathrm{d} v}{\mathrm{~d} t}=a \quad$ or $\quad \frac{\mathrm{d} v}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} s}{\mathrm{~d} t^{2}}$.
Accordingly displacement, velocity and acceleration are linked together by the process of differentiation with respect to time.

In reverse order, $a, v$ and $s$ are linked together by integration.
If we integrate an expression for the acceleration of a body with respect to time, we obtain an expression for the velocity of the body.

This is summarised diagramatically on the right.

## Differentiate with respect to time

displacement - velocity - acceleration

$$
s=a
$$

## Integrate with respect to time

## Example 1

A body moves in a straight line.
At time $t$ seconds, its acceleration is given by $a=6 t+1$.
When $t=0$, the velocity of the body is $2 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$ and its displacement from a fixed point O is 1 metre.
(i) Find expressions for $v$ and $s$ in terms of $t$.
(ii) Find the velocity of the body after 4 seconds.
(i) Given $a=6 t+1$, i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d} v}{\mathrm{~d} t}=6 t+1$

Velocity $(v)=\int(6 t+1) \mathrm{d} t$
$\therefore \quad v=\frac{6 t^{2}}{2}+t+c$, i.e. $v=3 t^{2}+t+c$
When $t=0, v=2$; thus, $2=0+0+c \quad \therefore \quad c=2$
Thus, $v=3 t^{2}+t+2 \quad \ldots c=2$

Using $\quad v=\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}=3 t^{2}+t+2$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Rightarrow \quad s & =\int\left(3 t^{2}+t+2\right) \mathrm{d} t \\
s & =\frac{3 t^{3}}{3}+\frac{t^{2}}{2}+2 t+c \\
s & =t^{3}+\frac{t^{2}}{2}+2 t+c
\end{aligned}
$$

When $t=0, s=1 \Rightarrow 1=0+c \Rightarrow c=1$
Thus, $s=t^{3}+\frac{t^{2}}{2}+2 t+1$
(ii) Velocity $=v=3 t^{2}+t+2$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Rightarrow v & =3(4)^{2}+(4)+2 \quad \ldots \text { when } t=4 \\
v & =54 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec} \text { when } t=4
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, the velocity $=54 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$ after 4 seconds.

## Exercise 3.3

In questions (1-5), $s$ metres represents displacement relative to a fixed point $\mathrm{O}, v \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$ represents velocity, and $a \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}^{2}$ represents acceleration.

1. A body starts from a fixed point O and moves in a straight line.

Its velocity $v$ is given by $v=5 t+4$ after $t$ seconds.
(i) If $s$ represents the distance from the origin, express $s$ in terms of $t$, given that $s=0$ when $t=0$.
(ii) Find the distance of the body from the origin after 4 seconds.
2. An object is travelling with a velocity of $v=\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}=t^{2}-4 t+3$.
(i) Find the acceleration when $t=5$.
(ii) If $s$ is the distance travelled, express $s$ in terms of $t$, given that $s=4$ when $t=3$.
(iii) Find the displacement when $t=1$.
3. The acceleration of a body is given by $a=6 t-12$.
(i) Find the velocity $v$ in terms of $t$, given that $v=9$ when $t=0$.
(ii) Find the displacement $s$ in terms of $t$, given that $s=6$ when $t=0$.
(iii) Find the values of $t$ when the body is at rest.
4. The acceleration of a particle can be modelled by the equation $a=(2 t-3) \mathrm{cm} / \mathrm{sec}^{2}$. If the particle starts 2 metres from the point O (moving away from O ) and its speed when $t=0$ is $3 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$, find
(i) the velocity, $v$, and displacement, $s$, in terms of $t$
(ii) the velocity and displacement when $t=2$ seconds.
5. A body is projected vertically upwards with an initial velocity of $25 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$. Its acceleration is $-10 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}^{2}$. Find
(i) the body's velocity at any time in terms of time $t$
(ii) its height, $s$, above the point of projection at any time
(iii) the time it takes to reach its maximum height
(iv) the maximum height reached
(v) the time taken to return to the point of projection.
6. $\frac{\mathrm{d} N}{\mathrm{~d} t}=4 \mathrm{e}^{t}+10$ represents the rate at which a colony of bacteria increases, where $N$ is the number of bacteria and $t$ is measured in hours.
(i) Find an expression for $N$ in terms of $t$.
(ii) If there were 10 bacteria in the colony initially, find the number in the colony after 5 hours, correct to the nearest whole number.
7. A train starts from rest at station A and $2 \frac{1}{2}$ minutes later, passes through station B. Its velocity, $t$ seconds after starting, is given by $v=0.6 t-0.004 t^{2}$, where $v$ is expressed in metres per second.
(i) Find an expression in $t$ for the distance travelled from A .
(ii) Find the distance between the two stations.
8. The rate at which water rises in a container is given by $\frac{\mathrm{d} h}{\mathrm{~d} t}=2 t-3$, where $h$ is measured in cm and $t$ is measured in seconds.
(i) Find a formula for the height of the water in the container after $t$ seconds, given that there was 4 cm of water in the container at the start.
(ii) Find the time taken to fill the container if it is
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-081.jpg?height=376&width=392&top_left_y=1203&top_left_x=1237) 36 cm in height.

## Section 3.4 Definite integrals

The diagram on the right shows the line $y=x$. The shaded triangle is the area of the region between the line $y=x$ and the $x$-axis between the values $x=0$ and $x=3$.
Thus, the area is $\frac{1}{2}(3)(3)=4 \frac{1}{2}$ square units.
The equation of the line is $y=x$ or $f(x)=x$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-081.jpg?height=313&width=402&top_left_y=1852&top_left_x=1131)

$$
\int f(x) \mathrm{d} x=\int x \mathrm{~d} x=\frac{x^{2}}{2}+c
$$

If we evaluate this integral at $x=3$ and $x=0$ and subtract the results, we get:

$$
\left[\frac{9}{2}+c\right]-\left[\frac{0}{2}+c\right]=\frac{9}{2}+c-c=\frac{9}{2}=4 \frac{1}{2}
$$

This result gives us the area of the shaded region already found by a different method on the previous page. It illustrates the use of integral calculus in finding the area between a line or curve and the $x$-axis.
$\int_{0}^{3} f(x) \mathrm{d} x$ is the area between the curve $y=f(x)$ and the $x$-axis from $x=0$ to $x=3$.
The area is found by evaluating the integral at $x=3$ and $x=0$ and then subtracting the results.

The area enclosed between the curve $y=f(x)$, the $x$-axis and the lines $x=a$ and $x=b$ is given by

$$
\text { Area }=\int_{a}^{b} y \mathrm{~d} x
$$

The area above may be written as
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-082.jpg?height=328&width=348&top_left_y=680&top_left_x=1283)

$$
\int_{a}^{b} f^{\prime}(x) \mathrm{d} x=[f(x)]_{a}^{b}=f(b)-f(a)
$$

Note: 1. $\int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x$ is called a definite integral since it gives a definite answer. $\int(2 x+3) \mathrm{d} x=x^{2}+3 x+c$ is called an indefinite integral as it contains an unknown constant $c$.
2. The numbers $a$ and $b$ are called the limits of integration, $a$ being the lower limit and $b$ the upper limit.
3. The constant of integration may be ignored in definite integrals since the two $c$ 's cancel out when evaluating $f(b)-f(\mathrm{a})$.

Areas under a curve will be dealt with more fully in the next section.

## Example 1

Evaluate
(i) $\int_{0}^{2} 3 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int_{2}^{4}\left(x^{2}-x+3\right) d x$
(iii) $\int_{4}^{9} \frac{1}{\sqrt{x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(i) $\int_{0}^{2} 3 x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=\left[\frac{3 x^{3}}{3}\right]_{0}^{2}=\left[x^{3}\right]_{0}^{2}$

$$
=\left[2^{3}\right]-[0]=8-0=8
$$

(ii) $\int_{2}^{4}\left(x^{2}-x+3\right) \mathrm{d} x=\left[\frac{x^{3}}{3}-\frac{x^{2}}{2}+3 x\right]_{2}^{4}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& =\left[\frac{64}{3}-\frac{16}{2}+12\right]-\left[\frac{8}{3}-\frac{4}{2}+6\right] \\
& =\frac{152}{6}-\frac{40}{6}=18 \frac{2}{3}
\end{aligned}
$$

(iii) $\int_{4}^{9} \frac{1}{\sqrt{x}} \mathrm{~d} x=\int_{4}^{9} x^{-\frac{1}{2}} \mathrm{~d} x=\left[\frac{x^{\frac{1}{2}}}{\frac{1}{2}}\right]_{4}^{9}=[2 \sqrt{x}]_{4}^{9}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
{[2 \sqrt{x}]_{4}^{9} } & =[2 \sqrt{9}]-[2 \sqrt{4}] \\
& =6-4=2
\end{aligned}
$$

## Example 2

Evaluate (i) $\int_{\frac{\pi}{4}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \cos 2 x \mathrm{~d} x \quad$ (ii) $\int_{2}^{5} 4 \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x \quad$ (iii) $\int_{0}^{2} 9^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(i) $\int_{\frac{\pi}{4}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \cos 2 x \mathrm{~d} x=\left[\frac{\sin 2 x}{2}\right]_{\frac{\pi}{4}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
=\left[\frac{\sin 2\left(\frac{\pi}{2}\right)}{2}\right]-\left[\frac{\sin 2\left(\frac{\pi}{4}\right)}{2}\right] & =\frac{\sin \pi}{2}-\frac{\sin \frac{\pi}{2}}{2} \\
& =0-\frac{1}{2}=-\frac{1}{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

(ii) $\int_{2}^{5} 4 \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=4 \int_{2}^{5} \mathrm{e}^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=4\left[\mathrm{e}^{x}\right]_{2}^{5}$

$$
=4\left[\mathrm{e}^{5}-\mathrm{e}^{2}\right]
$$

(iii) $\int_{0}^{2} 9^{x} \mathrm{~d} x=\left[\frac{9^{x}}{\ln 9}\right]_{0}^{2}=\left[\frac{9^{2}}{\ln 9}\right]-\left[\frac{9^{0}}{\ln 9}\right]$

$$
=\left[\frac{81}{\ln 9}\right]-\left[\frac{1}{\ln 9}\right]=\frac{81}{\ln 9}-\frac{1}{\ln 9}=\frac{80}{\ln 9}
$$

## Exercise 3.4

Evaluate the definite integrals in numbers (1-15):

1. $\int_{1}^{2} 6 x \mathrm{~d} x$
2. $\int_{1}^{3}\left(3 x^{2}-2 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
3. $\int_{1}^{4}\left(3 x^{2}-4\right) d x$
4. $\int_{1}^{2}\left(x^{3}+2 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$
5. $\int_{1}^{3}\left(x^{2}-x+1\right) d x$
6. $\int_{-1}^{2}(2 x-5) d x$
7. $\int_{0}^{1} x^{2}(3-x) \mathrm{d} x$
8. $\int_{1}^{9} \sqrt{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
9. $\int_{2}^{4} \frac{1}{x^{2}} \mathrm{~d} x$
10. $\int_{4}^{9} \frac{\mathrm{~d} x}{\sqrt{x}}$
11. $\int_{0}^{2} \frac{x^{3}-2 x^{2}+4 x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
12. $\int_{1}^{4}(\sqrt{x}-2)^{2} \mathrm{~d} x$
13. $\int_{-2}^{-1} \frac{2}{x^{3}} \mathrm{~d} x$
14. $\int_{1}^{16} \frac{\sqrt{x}-4}{\sqrt{x}} \mathrm{~d} x$
15. $\int_{1}^{4}\left(\sqrt{x}+\frac{1}{\sqrt{x}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$
16. Show that $\int_{1}^{2}(x-1)(x-2) \mathrm{d} x=-\frac{1}{6}$.
17. Use factors to simplify the expression $\frac{x^{2}-16}{2 x+8}$. Hence, evaluate $\int_{0}^{1} \frac{x^{2}-16}{2 x+8} \mathrm{~d} x$.
18. If $\int_{0}^{k}(2 x-4) \mathrm{d} x=-3$, find the values of $k$ if $k>0$.
19. If $\int_{0}^{k}\left(x^{2}-3 x\right) \mathrm{d} x=0$, find the value of $k$ if $k>0$.
20. Factorise $x^{3}-8$ and hence evaluate $\int_{0}^{2} \frac{x^{3}-8}{x-2} \mathrm{~d} x$.
21. Find the value of $n$ such that $\int_{0}^{1} n x^{2} \mathrm{~d} x=1$.
22. Evaluate each of these definite integrals:
(i) $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{4}} \cos 2 x \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{6}} \sin 3 x d x$
(iii) $\int_{\frac{\pi}{3}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} 5 \sin x \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{2}}(2 \cos x+1) d x$
23. Evaluate each of the following:
(i) $\int_{0}^{2} \mathrm{e}^{4 x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(ii) $\int_{-1}^{1} \mathrm{e}^{x+3} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int_{0}^{1} \mathrm{e}^{\frac{x}{2}} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int_{0}^{1}\left(\mathrm{e}^{-2 x}+1\right) \mathrm{d} x$
24. Find the value of each of these integrals:
(i) $\int_{0}^{1}\left(2 \mathrm{e}^{\frac{x}{3}}+2\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int_{-2}^{2} \frac{\mathrm{e}^{x}+\mathrm{e}^{-x}}{2} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int_{1}^{3} 5^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iv) $\int_{0}^{\mathrm{e}} 7^{x} \mathrm{~d} x$
25. If $f(x)=\frac{\cos x}{\sin x}$, show that $f^{\prime}(x)=\frac{-1}{\sin ^{2} x}$.

Hence, find $\int_{\frac{\pi}{4}}^{\frac{\pi}{2}} \frac{1}{\sin ^{2} x} \mathrm{~d} x$.
26. Find $\frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d} x}(x \sin 3 x)$.

Hence, evaluate $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{6}} 3 x \cos 3 x \mathrm{~d} x$.

## Section 3.5 Finding areas by integration

In this section, we will show how integration can be used to find the area, $A$, between a curve $y=f(x)$ and the $x$-axis from $x=a$ to $x=b$, as represented by the shaded area in the diagram on the right.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-085.jpg?height=345&width=580&top_left_y=264&top_left_x=1049)

One way to estimate the area would be to divide it into a large number of rectangular strips, as shown below. Since each of these strips approximates to a rectangle, the sum of the areas of those rectangles gives an approximate value for $A$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-085.jpg?height=472&width=473&top_left_y=806&top_left_x=367)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-085.jpg?height=472&width=465&top_left_y=806&top_left_x=998)

We will now consider one such rectangle, as shown on the right above.
Let the area under the curve from $x=a$ to $x=x$ be $A(x)$, and the area from $x=a$ to $x=x+h$ be $A(x+h)$.
The area of the narrow (shaded) strip is approximately equal to $h . f(x)$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow A(x+h)-A(x) \approx f(x) \cdot h \\
& \Rightarrow \frac{A(x+h)-A(x)}{h} \approx f(x)
\end{aligned}
$$

As $h \rightarrow 0 \Rightarrow \lim _{h \rightarrow 0} \frac{A(x+h)-A(x)}{h}=f(x)$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} x}=f(x) \\
& \Rightarrow \mathrm{d} A=f(x) \mathrm{d} x \\
& \Rightarrow A=\int f(x) \mathrm{d} x
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus the integral of the function $y=f(x)$ gives the area function $A(x)$.
If we substitute $b$ and $a$ for $x$, we get the area between the curve, the $x$-axis and the lines $x=b$ and $x=a$.

Area under a Curve

The area, $A$, of the region between the curve $y=f(x)$ and the $x$-axis between the lines $x=a$ and $x=b$ is given by

$$
A=\int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-086.jpg?height=317&width=520&top_left_y=221&top_left_x=1074)

When using $\int_{a}^{b} y \mathrm{~d} x$ to find the area between a curve and the $x$-axis, the areas of the regions above and below the $x$-axis must be found separately. If $b>a$, the value of $\int_{a}^{b} y \mathrm{~d} x$ will be positive if the area enclosed is above the $x$-axis, and negative if the area is below the $x$-axis.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-086.jpg?height=350&width=550&top_left_y=664&top_left_x=1083)

If an area is -16 , we take the absolute value, 16 , to be the area.

## Area between a curve and the $\boldsymbol{y}$-axis

If we require the area between a curve and the $y$-axis, the function must be written in the form $\boldsymbol{x}=\boldsymbol{f}(\boldsymbol{y})$.

The area of the shaded region between the curve and the $y$-axis between the lines $y=b$ and $y=a$ is given by:

## Area between a curve and the $y$-axis

$$
\text { Area } A=\int_{a}^{b} x \mathrm{~d} y
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-086.jpg?height=407&width=413&top_left_y=1251&top_left_x=1222)

If the region is to the right of the $y$-axis, the area is positive; if the region is to the left of the $y$-axis, the area is negative.

Areas to the right and to the left of the $y$-axis must be found separately and then added.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-086.jpg?height=352&width=331&top_left_y=1752&top_left_x=1301)

## Example 1

Find the area of the shaded region shown in the given diagram.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Area } & =\int_{2}^{4}\left(x^{2}-4\right) \mathrm{d} x+\int_{1}^{2}\left(x^{2}-4\right) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\left[\frac{x^{3}}{3}-4 x\right]_{2}^{4}+\left[\frac{x^{3}}{3}-4 x\right]_{1}^{2} \\
& =\frac{64}{3}-16-\left(\frac{8}{3}-8\right)+\left[\left(\frac{8}{3}-8\right)-\left(\frac{1}{3}-4\right)\right] \\
& =\frac{64}{3}-16-\left(-\frac{16}{3}\right)+\left[-\frac{16}{3}-\left(-\frac{11}{3}\right)\right] \\
& =\frac{64}{3}-16+\frac{16}{3}+\left[-\frac{16}{3}+\frac{11}{3}\right] \\
& =\frac{80}{3}-16+\left[-\frac{5}{3}\right] \quad \ldots \text { since area below the } x \text {-axis is negative, we take the absolute } \\
& =\frac{32}{3}+\left|-\frac{5}{3}\right|=\frac{32}{3}+\frac{5}{3}=\frac{37}{3} \text { square units. }
\end{aligned}
$$

## Area between two curves

The given figure shows two curves $y=f(x)$ and $y=g(x)$ intersecting at the points where $x=a$ and $x=b$.

The shaded area $=\int_{a}^{b} g(x) \mathrm{d} x-\int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-087.jpg?height=430&width=536&top_left_y=1466&top_left_x=1095)

## Example 2

Find the area of the region bounded by the curve $y=-x^{2}+5 x-4$ and the line $y=x-1$.

The curve $y=-x^{2}+5 x-4$ represents a quadratic curve with a maximum turning point as the coefficient of $x^{2}$ is negative.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y=0 & \Rightarrow-x^{2}+5 x-4=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}-5 x+4=0 \\
& \Rightarrow(x-4)(x-1)=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x=4 \text { or } x=1
\end{aligned}
$$

$\therefore \quad$ The curve intersects the $x$-axis at $x=4$ and at $x=1$.
A rough sketch of the curve is shown.
We use simultaneous equations to find
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-088.jpg?height=380&width=585&top_left_y=522&top_left_x=957) the point of intersection of the curve and the line.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y & =-x^{2}+5 x-4 \text { and } y=x-1 \\
& \Rightarrow-x^{2}+5 x-4=x-1 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}-4 x+3=0 \\
& \Rightarrow(x-3)(x-1)=0 \Rightarrow x=3 \text { or } x=1
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, the curve intersects the line at the points where $x=1$ and $x=3$.
(There is no need to find the corresponding $y$-values.)
The required shaded area is the area between the curve and the $x$-axis between $x=3$ and $x=1$ less the area between the line and the $x$-axis between $x=3$ and $x=1$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Area } & =\int_{1}^{3}\left(-x^{2}+5 x-4\right) \mathrm{d} x-\int_{1}^{3}(x-1) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\left[-\frac{x^{3}}{3}+\frac{5 x^{2}}{2}-4 x\right]_{1}^{3}-\left[\frac{x^{2}}{2}-x\right]_{1}^{3} \\
& =\left[\left(-9+\frac{45}{2}-12\right)-\left(-\frac{1}{3}+\frac{5}{2}-4\right)\right]-\left[\left(\frac{9}{2}-3\right)-\left(\frac{1}{2}-1\right)\right] \\
& =\left[\frac{3}{2}+\frac{11}{6}\right]-\left[\frac{3}{2}+\frac{1}{2}\right] \\
& =\frac{4}{3} \text { sq. units }
\end{aligned}
$$

## Example 3

The diagram on the right shows a sketch of the function $y=\frac{2}{x^{2}}$.
The shaded region represents the area bounded by the curve and the $x$-axis between the lines $x=3$ and $x=1$.

If the line $x=k$ divides this area into two equal portions, find the value of $k$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-089.jpg?height=435&width=503&top_left_y=275&top_left_x=1106)

$$
\begin{aligned}
& A_{1}=A_{2} \\
\Rightarrow & \int_{k}^{3} \frac{2}{x^{2}} \mathrm{~d} x=\int_{1}^{k} \frac{2}{x^{2}} \mathrm{~d} x \\
\Rightarrow & \int_{k}^{3} 2 x^{-2} \mathrm{~d} x=\int_{1}^{k} 2 x^{-2} \mathrm{~d} x \\
\Rightarrow & {\left[\frac{2 x^{-1}}{-1}\right]_{k}^{3}=\left[\frac{2 x^{-1}}{-1}\right]_{1}^{k} } \\
\Rightarrow & {\left[-\frac{2}{x}\right]_{k}^{3}=\left[-\frac{2}{x}\right]_{1}^{k} } \\
\Rightarrow & \left(-\frac{2}{3}\right)-\left(-\frac{2}{k}\right)=\left(-\frac{2}{k}\right)-\left(-\frac{2}{1}\right) \\
\Rightarrow & -\frac{2}{3}+\frac{2}{k}=-\frac{2}{k}+2 \\
\Rightarrow & \frac{4}{k}=2 \frac{2}{3} \Rightarrow \frac{4}{k}=\frac{8}{3} \Rightarrow 8 k=12 \Rightarrow k=\frac{3}{2}
\end{aligned}
$$

## Section 3.6 Average value of a function

In some cases it is useful to know the average value of a function, especially if the function is periodic.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-093.jpg?height=261&width=508&top_left_y=1423&top_left_x=1123)

The average value of a continuous function over a given interval (e.g. $f(x)=6+6 x^{2}-x^{3}$ from $x=1$ to $x=5$ ) is defined as the height of a rectangle standing on the same interval ( $5-1=4$ ) which has the same area as the area trapped between the function and the $x$-axis.
i.e. Average value $\times 4=\int_{1}^{5}\left(6+6 x^{2}-x^{3}\right) \mathrm{d} x=116$
$\Rightarrow$ average value of $f(x)$, from $x=1$ to $x=5$,

$$
=116 \div 4=29
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-093.jpg?height=491&width=569&top_left_y=1730&top_left_x=1062)

In general terms the average value of $y=f(x)$, over an interval of $[a, b]$ is defined as the height of the rectangle that has the same area as the area between the $x$-axis and the curve for the interval $[a, b]$.
The exact average value can be found using integration.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-094.jpg?height=310&width=486&top_left_y=170&top_left_x=1143)

The average value of a function $f(x)$ over an interval $[a, b]$ is

$$
\text { Average value }=\frac{1}{b-a} \int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x
$$

## Example 1

The graph of the function, $f(x)=x^{2}-4 x+5$ is shown. Find the average value of the function for $1 \leqslant x \leqslant 4$.
Draw a horizontal line to represent this average value and explain its meaning.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Average value } & =\frac{1}{b-a} \int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\frac{1}{4-1} \int_{1}^{4}\left(x^{2}-4 x+5\right) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\frac{1}{3}\left[\frac{x^{3}}{3}-\frac{4 x^{2}}{2}+5 x\right]_{1}^{4}=\frac{1}{3}\left[\frac{x^{3}}{3}-2 x^{2}+5 x\right]_{1}^{4} \\
& =\frac{1}{3}\left[\left(\frac{64}{3}-32+20\right)-\left(\frac{1}{3}-2+5\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
=\frac{1}{3}\left[\left(9 \frac{1}{3}\right)-\left(3 \frac{1}{3}\right)\right]
$$

$$
=\frac{1}{3}[6]=2
$$

$\therefore \quad$ The average value $=2$
The line $y=2$ represents the average value. The shaded rectangle is equal in area to the area between the curve and the $x$-axis from $x=1$ to $x=4$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-094.jpg?height=362&width=408&top_left_y=889&top_left_x=1135)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-094.jpg?height=465&width=557&top_left_y=1519&top_left_x=985)

## Example 2

A body starts from rest and moves in a straight line.
After $t$ seconds its velocity ( $v \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{s}$ ) is given by $v=2 t-4, t \geqslant 0$.
(i) By completing the table on the right, estimate the average velocity over the first 3 seconds.

| $\boldsymbol{t}=$ | 0 | 1 | 2 | 3 |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| $\boldsymbol{v}=$ |  |  |  |  |

(ii) Use integration to test the accuracy of your answer.
(i)

| $\boldsymbol{t}=$ | 0 | 1 | 2 | 3 |
| :--- | :---: | :---: | :--- | :--- |
| $\boldsymbol{v}=$ | -4 | -2 | 0 | 2 |

Average velocity $=\frac{-4-2+0+2}{4}=-1 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec} \quad \ldots$ although $t=3$ seconds, four readings were taken.
(ii) The average value of a function $f(x)$ is $\frac{1}{b-a} \int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Average velocity } & =\frac{1}{3-0} \int_{0}^{3}(2 t-4) \mathrm{d} t \\
& =\frac{1}{3}\left[\frac{2 t^{2}}{2}-4 t\right]_{0}^{3}=\frac{1}{3}\left[t^{2}-4 t\right]_{0}^{3} \\
& =\frac{1}{3}\left[\left(3^{2}-4(3)\right)-(0-0)\right] \\
& =\frac{1}{3}[9-12]=\frac{1}{3}[-3]=-1 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{s}
\end{aligned}
$$

In this case, the average velocity is also $-1 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{s}$.
This is the true average velocity; the first method only gives an estimate.
The estimate in (i) obtained by sampling was accurate in this example but for most functions is not.

## Example 3

The average value of the function $f(x)=2 x+3$ for $1 \leqslant x \leqslant k$ is 11 .
Find the value of $k$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Average value } & =\frac{1}{b-a} \int_{a}^{b} f(x) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\frac{1}{k-1} \int_{a}^{b}(2 x+3) \mathrm{d} x \\
& =\frac{1}{k-1}\left[\frac{2 x^{2}}{2}+3 x\right]_{1}^{k} \\
& =\frac{1}{k-1}\left[\left(k^{2}+3 k\right)-(1+3)\right] \\
& =\frac{1}{k-1}\left[k^{2}+3 k-4\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

$\therefore \quad \frac{1}{k-1}\left(k^{2}+3 k-4\right)=11 \quad \ldots$ the average value, 11, is given.

$$
\begin{array}{ll}
\therefore \quad & k^{2}+3 k-4=11(k-1) \\
& k^{2}-8 k+7=0 \\
& (k-7)(k-1)=0 \\
& k=7 \text { or } k=1 \\
\therefore \quad & k=7 \quad \ldots k=1 \text { is the second limit, already given. }
\end{array}
$$

## Exercise 3.6

1. On the right is the graph of the function $f(x)=-x^{2}+4 x+12$.
(i) By reading the values of $f(0), f(1), \ldots f(6)$, estimate the average value of the function in the interval $0 \leqslant x \leqslant 6$.
(ii) Now use integration to find the exact average value of $f(x)$ in the interval $[0,6]$.
(iii) Find the percentage error in using the estimate.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-096.jpg?height=506&width=508&top_left_y=1489&top_left_x=1123)
2. Find the average value of each of these functions in the given interval:
(i) $f(x)=2 x-4$ in the interval $2 \leqslant x \leqslant 5$.
(ii) $f(x)=x^{2}-x$ in the interval $0 \leqslant x \leqslant 2$.
(iii) $f(x)=2 x-x^{2}$ in the interval $0 \leqslant x \leqslant 2$.
3. Find the average value of the function $f(x)=x^{3}$ for $0 \leqslant x \leqslant 4$.
4. Find the average value of the function $f(x)=x^{2}+4$ for $-2 \leqslant x \leqslant 3$.
5. Find the average value of each of the following functions for the stated intervals:
(i) $f(x)=\sin x, x \in\left[0, \frac{\pi}{2}\right]$
(ii) $f(x)=\cos x, x \in[0,2 \pi]$
(iii) $f(x)=\mathrm{e}^{x}, x \in[0,3]$
(iv) $f(x)=\mathrm{e}^{4 x}, x \in[0,2]$
6. The average value of the function $f(x)=x+1$ for $2 \leqslant x \leqslant k$ is 8 . Find the value of $k$.
7. The average value of the function $f(x)=x^{3}$ for $0 \leqslant x \leqslant k$ is 16 . Find the value of $k$.
8. Find the average value of each of these functions in the given interval:
(i) $f(x)=\frac{1}{x^{2}}$, for $1 \leqslant x \leqslant 5$
(ii) $f(x)=5 \cos \left(\frac{x}{2}\right)$, for $0 \leqslant x \leqslant 2 \pi$
9. The volume, $V \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, of water in a conical vessel is given by $V=\frac{\pi h^{3}}{12}$, where $h \mathrm{~cm}$ is the depth of the water.
Find the average volume of water in the vessel as the depth increases from 2 cm to 8 cm .
10. A body falls from rest. Its velocity in metres per second at time $t$ seconds is given by $v=9.8 t$. Find the average velocity of the body over the first 3 seconds of its motion.
11. The velocity $v$ in $\mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}$ of a body $t$ seconds after timing commences is given by $v=3 t^{2}-4$.
(i) Find the average velocity during the interval $t=1$ to $t=3$.
(ii) Find the average acceleration during the interval $t=1$ to $t=3$.
12. A particle moves with velocity $v=5-(t-3)^{2}$, where $v$ is measured in metres/sec.
(i) Find the average velocity of the particle in the first six seconds.
(ii) At what time(s) will it have this average velocity?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-097.jpg?height=378&width=565&top_left_y=1650&top_left_x=986)
13. The tension $T$ newtons in a particular spring depends on the extension $x$ metres of the spring from its natural length in accordance with the rule $T=30 x$. Find the average tension in the spring as $x$ increases from 0.1 m to 0.2 m .
14. Given the curve whose equation is $y=x^{-\frac{1}{2}}$, find
(i) the average value of $\frac{1}{y}$, with respect to $x$, in the interval $1 \leqslant x \leqslant 4$
(ii) the area of the region $R$ bounded by the curve, the $x$-axis and the lines $x=1$ and $x=4$.
15. A mass of gas of volume $v$ at pressure $p$ expands according to the law $p v^{\frac{3}{4}}=30$. Find the average pressure as the gas expands from $v=1$ to $v=16$.
16. An object is fired vertically upwards so that after time $t$, its velocity is given by $v=40-10 t$, where $v$ is in metres per second.
Find the average velocity between $t=1$ and $t=3$.

## Revision Exercise 3 (Core)

1. Find each of the following integrals:
(i) $\int(2 x+5) \mathrm{d} x$

(ii) $\int\left(e^{2 x}+e^{-x}\right) \mathrm{d} x$

(i) $\int_{0}^{3}\left(2 x^{2}-4 x+1\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{4}} \cos 2 x d x$
(iii) $\int_{0}^{\frac{\pi}{3}}(\cos 3 \theta+\sin 3 \theta) d \theta$

(i) $\int_{0}^{3}\left(e^{2 x}+1\right) \mathrm{d} x$
(ii) $\int_{0}^{2} 2 e^{-2 x} \mathrm{~d} x$
(iii) $\int_{1}^{2}\left(e^{2 x}+\frac{4}{x^{2}}\right) \mathrm{d} x$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-099.jpg?height=307&width=404&top_left_y=179&top_left_x=1227)

Find an expression for $y$ in terms of $x$.

## Revision Exercise 3 (Advanced)

1. (i) Evaluate $\int_{1}^{3}\left(9 x^{2}-4 x\right) \mathrm{d} x$.
(ii) If $\int_{0}^{a}\left(9 x^{2}-4 x\right) \mathrm{d} x=0$, find the possible value of $a$ when $a>0$.
2. Find the average value of the function $f(x)=(x+3)(2 x-5)$ in the interval [1,5].
3. The figure shows the curve $y=x^{2}$ and the straight line $2 x+y=15$.
(i) Find the coordinates of P and Q .
(ii) Find the area of the shaded region.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-100.jpg?height=315&width=510&top_left_y=177&top_left_x=1122)
4. The volume, $V \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, of water in a hemispherical bowl is given by $V=\frac{1}{3} \pi\left(30 h^{2}-h^{3}\right)$, where $h \mathrm{~cm}$ is the depth of the water.
Find the average volume of water in the bowl as the depth increases from 0 to 4 cm .
5. Part of the curve $y=x^{3}-3 x^{2}+5$ is given. The point A is a local maximum point of the curve and $\ell$ is a tangent to the curve at A . Find the coordinates of A and hence find the area between the line $\ell$ and the curve.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-100.jpg?height=320&width=501&top_left_y=764&top_left_x=1133)
6. Given $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=a e^{-x}+1$ and also $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=3$ when $x=0$.

If $y=5$ when $x=0$, find the value of $y$ when $x=2$.
7. The figure shows part of the curve $y=\frac{10}{x^{2}}$.
(i) Find the area of region $A$.
(ii) Find the value of P for which the regions $B$ and $C$ are of equal area.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-100.jpg?height=329&width=609&top_left_y=1361&top_left_x=1023)

$$
f(x)=25-0.02 x^{2}, \text { for }-20 \leqslant x \leqslant 20 .
$$

The hall is 80 metres long.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-100.jpg?height=343&width=506&top_left_y=1776&top_left_x=1126) may be installed.

## Applications of Differential Calculus

## Key words

increasing function decreasing function stationary points local maximum local minimum point of inflection slope function rate of change velocity acceleration related rates of change

## Section 4.1 Tangents - Increasing and decreasing functions

In chapter 7 book 4, it was stated that for any function $y=f(x)$, the derived function $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ can be interpreted geometrically as the slope of the tangent to the curve at any point on the curve.

If $y=f(x)$, then $f^{\prime}(a)$ gives the slope of the tangent to the curve at the point where $x=a$.
Thus, to find the equation of the tangent to a curve at the point $\left(x_{1}, y_{1}\right)$ :
(i) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
(ii) Evaluate $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ at $\left(x_{1}, y_{1}\right)$.
(iii) Use the equation

$$
y-y_{1}=m\left(x-x_{1}\right)
$$

to find the equation of the tangent at the point $\left(x_{1}, y_{1}\right)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-105.jpg?height=373&width=496&top_left_y=1273&top_left_x=914)

## Example 1

Find the equation of the tangent to the curve $y=\frac{x^{2}}{3}-x+1$ at the point $\left(4, \frac{7}{3}\right)$.
To find the slope of the tangent to the curve, we first find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y=\frac{x^{2}}{3}-x+1 \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x} & =\frac{2 x}{3}-1 \\
& =\frac{2(4)}{3}-1=\frac{5}{3} \quad \ldots \text { at } x=4
\end{aligned}
$$

Equation of tangent at $\left(4, \frac{7}{3}\right)$ :

$$
\begin{aligned}
& y-y_{1}=m\left(x-x_{1}\right) \\
& y-\frac{7}{3}=\frac{5}{3}(x-4) \\
& \Rightarrow 3 y-7=5 x-20 \\
& \Rightarrow 5 x-3 y-13=0 \text { is the required equation. }
\end{aligned}
$$

## Example 2

At what points on the curve $y=x^{3}-9 x^{2}+20 x-8$ is the tangent parallel to the line $4 x+y-3=0$ ?

The slope of the line $4 x+y-3=0$ is $-4 . \quad \ldots y=-4 x+3$
Any line parallel to $4 x+y-3=0$ will have slope -4 .
We find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ to find the slope of any tangent to $y=x^{3}-9 x^{2}+20 x-8$.

$$
\frac{d y}{d x}=3 x^{2}-18 x+20
$$

For slope $-4, \quad 3 x^{2}-18 x+20=-4$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \Rightarrow 3 x^{2}-18 x+24=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}-6 x+8=0 \\
& \Rightarrow(x-2)(x-4)=0 \Rightarrow x=2 \text { or } x=4
\end{aligned}
$$

We now find the corresponding $y$-values for $x=2$ and $x=4$.

$$
x=2 \Rightarrow y=(2)^{3}-9(2)^{2}+20(2)-8 \Rightarrow y=4
$$

$\therefore(2,4)$ is one point where the slope is -4 .

$$
x=4 \Rightarrow y=(4)^{3}-9(4)^{2}+20(4)-8 \Rightarrow y=-8
$$

$\therefore(4,-8)$ is a second point where the slope is -4
$\therefore$ The two required points are $(2,4)$ and $(4,-8)$.

## Increasing and decreasing functions

Examining the given curve from left to right, we can see that it is rising (or increasing) from $A$ to $B$, and decreasing from $B$ to $C$.
From A to B, the slope of the tangent (i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ ) is positive.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-106.jpg?height=270&width=564&top_left_y=1916&top_left_x=1071)

From B to C , the slope of the tangent is negative.

When the curve is increasing, $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}>0$.
When the curve is decreasing, $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}<0$.

## Example 3

Find the interval for which the function $f(x)=x^{3}-3 x^{2}-9 x+9$ is
(i) increasing
(ii) decreasing
(i) When $f(x)$ is increasing, $f^{\prime}(x)>0$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
f^{\prime}(x)=3 x^{2}-6 x-9 & \Rightarrow 3 x^{2}-6 x-9>0 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}-2 x-3>0 \\
& \Rightarrow(x-3)(x+1)>0
\end{aligned}
$$

Using a rough sketch of the quadratic function $y=x^{2}-2 x-3$, we can see that:
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-107.jpg?height=315&width=408&top_left_y=762&top_left_x=1135)
$(x-3)(x+1)>0 \Rightarrow x>3$ and $x<-1$.
Therefore, $f(x)$ is increasing when $x>3$ and $x<-1$.
(ii) When $f(x)$ is decreasing, $f^{\prime}(x)$ is negative.

From the graph, $f^{\prime}(x)$ is negative when $(x-3)(x+1)<0$, i.e. when $x>-1$ and $x<3$.
$\therefore f(x)$ is decreasing in the interval $-1<x<3$.

## Example 4

Show that the curve $y=x^{3}+3 x^{2}+6 x$ is increasing for all values of $x \in R$.
If a curve is increasing, then $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ is positive.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y=x^{3}+3 x^{2}+6 x \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x} & =3 x^{2}+6 x+6 \\
& =3\left(x^{2}+2 x+2\right) \\
& =3\left(x^{2}+2 x+1+1\right) \\
& =3\left[(x+1)^{2}+1\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

Since $(x+1)^{2}, x \in R$ is always positive, then $3\left[(x+1)^{2}+1\right]$ is positive.
Therefore, the curve is increasing for all $x \in R$ as $3\left[(x+1)^{2}+1\right]$ is positive for all $x \in R$.
