## Section 4.2 Stationary points

In the diagram below, the tangents to the curve at the points A and B are parallel to the $x$-axis, and so the slope of each tangent is zero.
Points on a curve at which $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ is zero are called stationary points.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-110.jpg?height=465&width=1234&top_left_y=1003&top_left_x=302)

In the curve above, A and B are stationary points and they are also turning points.
The turning point at A is called a local maximum point, since the value of the function at this point exceeds all values of the function immediately to the right or left of A .

A maximum value of a function is not necessarily the greatest value of the function.
This is illustrated in the curve above where the value of the function at C is greater than the value of the function at A .

The turning point at B above is called a minimum turning point or simply a local minimum.
The following steps should be used to find the turning points of a curve:
(i) find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ of the function
(ii) let $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$ and solve the equation
(iii) for each value of $x$, find the corresponding value for $y$.

## Example 1

Find the turning points of the curve $y=x+\frac{1}{x}$.
To find the turning points, we solve the equation $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& y=x+\frac{1}{x} \Rightarrow y=x+x^{-1} \\
& \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=1-\frac{1}{x^{2}} \\
& \begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0 \Rightarrow 1-\frac{1}{x^{2}}=0 & \Rightarrow x^{2}-1=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}=1 \Rightarrow x= \pm 1
\end{aligned} \\
& x=1 \Rightarrow y=2 \text { and } x=-1 \Rightarrow y=-2
\end{aligned}
$$

$\therefore$ The turning points of the curve are $(1,2)$ and $(-1,-2)$.

## Determining the nature of a turning point

In the diagram below, the positive signs (+) indicate where the slope of the curve is positive (i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}>0$ ), and the negative signs indicate where the slope of the curve is negative $\left(\right.$ i.e. $\left.\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}<0\right)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-111.jpg?height=336&width=720&top_left_y=1218&top_left_x=559)

As the point A is approached from the left, the slope of the curve is positive but decreasing; at A , the slope is zero, and to the right of A , the slope is negative. Thus, as we go through the point A , the slope of the curve changes from positive to negative (i.e. is decreasing).
Thus, the rate of change of $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ (i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$ ) is negative.
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$ is negative at a maximum turning point.
Similarly, at the point B , the slope of the curve changes from negative to positive, that is, it is increasing.
Thus, the rate of change of $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}\left(\right.$ i.e. $\left.\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}\right)$ is positive at the point B .
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$ is positive at a minimum turning point.

The second derivative test for turning points

If, $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}<0$, i.e. is negative, at a point, then the point is a local maximum point.
If, $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}>0$, i.e. is positive, at a point, then the point is a local minimum point.

## Example 2

Find the turning points of the curve $y=x^{3}-9 x^{2}+15 x$ and determine the nature of these turning points.
To find the turning points, we solve the equation $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y=x^{3}-9 x^{2}+15 x & \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=3 x^{2}-18 x+15 \\
\frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0 & \Rightarrow 3 x^{2}-18 x+15=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}-6 x+5=0 \\
& \Rightarrow(x-5)(x-1)=0 \\
& \Rightarrow x=5 \text { or } x=1
\end{aligned}
$$

When $x=5, y=(5)^{3}-9(5)^{2}+15(5) \Rightarrow y=-25$
$\therefore(5,-25)$ is a turning point.
When $x=1, y=(1)^{3}-9(1)^{2}+15(1)=7$
$\therefore(1,7)$ is also a turning point.
To determine the nature of these turning points, we find $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$.
$\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=3 x^{2}-18 x+15 \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=6 x-18$
At $x=5, \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=6(5)-18=12$, which is positive.
$\Rightarrow(5,-25)$ is a minimum turning point.
At $x=1, \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=6(1)-18=-12$, which is negative.
$\Rightarrow(1,7)$ is a maximum turning point.

## Points of Inflection

The curve traced in the diagram below is said to be concave upwards from the point A to the point $B$, and concave downwards from the point $B$ to the point $C$.

The point B , where the curve changes from being concave upwards to concave downwards, is called a point of inflection.

At a point of inflection, the tangent to the curve crosses the curve.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-112.jpg?height=279&width=401&top_left_y=1961&top_left_x=1228)

At a point of inflection, B , of a curve $y=f(x), \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=0$ and changes sign as the curve passes through B.

At a point of inflection, the slope of the tangent to the curve need not be zero as shown in the diagram on the previous page.

## Point of Inflection

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { At a point of inflection, } \mathrm{B} \text {, on the curve } y=f(x), \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=0 \text { and } \\
& \text { changes sign as the curve passes through } \mathrm{B} \text {. }
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, to find the point(s) of inflection of a curve:
(i) find $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$
(ii) solve the equation $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=0$
(iii) for each value of $x$, find the corresponding value of $y$.

Note: The point B on the given curve is a point of inflection. The tangent to the curve at B is also parallel to the $x$-axis.
The point B is called a saddle point or a horizontal point of inflection and is another example of a stationary point.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-113.jpg?height=341&width=534&top_left_y=1140&top_left_x=1098)

## Example 3

Find the point of inflection of the curve $y=x^{3}-3 x^{2}-2$.
$y=x^{3}-3 x^{2}-2$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=3 x^{2}-6 x \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=6 x-6$
$\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=0 \Rightarrow 6 x-6=0 \Rightarrow x=1$
When $x=1, y=(1)^{3}-3(1)^{2}-2=-4$
$\therefore(1,-4)$ is the point of inflection.

## Example 4

Verify that the curve $y=\frac{x+2}{2 x-3}$ has no turning points or points of inflection.
Verify also that the curve is decreasing for all values of $x \in R$.
To find the turning points, we solve the equation $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
y=\frac{x+2}{2 x-3} \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x} & =\frac{(2 x-3)(1)-(x+2)(2)}{(2 x-3)^{2}} \\
& =\frac{2 x-3-2 x-4}{(2 x-3)^{2}}=\frac{-7}{(2 x-3)^{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

The expression $\frac{-7}{(2 x-3)^{2}}$ can never be zero, as a fraction is zero only if

## the numerator is zero.

Since $\frac{-7}{(2 x-3)^{2}} \neq 0$, the curve has no turning points.
To find the point of inflection, we solve the equation $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=0$.
$\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\frac{-7}{(2 x-3)^{2}}=-7(2 x-3)^{-2}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=14(2 x-3)^{-3}(2)=\frac{28}{(2 x-3)^{3}}$
Since $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=\frac{28}{(2 x-3)^{3}} \neq 0$, the curve has no point of inflection.
A curve is decreasing if $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ is negative.
$\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\frac{-7}{(2 x-3)^{2}}$, which is always negative as $(2 x-3)^{2}$ is always positive.
$\Rightarrow$ The curve is decreasing for all $x \in R$.

## Section 4.3 Graphs of the derived (or slope) function

The curve on the right is the graph of the function

$$
y=x^{2}-2 x-3
$$

$\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ for this function is $2 x-2$.
$\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=2 x-2$ is the derived or slope function.
The blue line represents this slope function.
From the two graphs, the following can be observed:
(i) From A to B on the curve, the slope is negative. For this $x$-interval, the slope function (blue line) is also negative, i.e. the blue line is below the
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-116.jpg?height=553&width=547&top_left_y=1282&top_left_x=1082) $x$-axis.
(ii) From B to C on the curve, the slope is positive.

For this $x$-interval, the slope function (blue line) is also positive, i.e. the blue line is above the $x$-axis.
(iii) The slope of the curve at the point B is zero, i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$.

At the point $\mathrm{B}, x=1$.
Notice that the slope function is also zero at the point where $x=1$.
This illustrates that $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ (the slope function) is zero at a turning point.

## The slope function of a cubic function

The red curve on the right is the graph of a cubic function, $y=f(x)$.
The blue curve is the graph of its slope function, $y=g(x)$.
Some of the features of these curves are:
$>$ In the function $y=f(x)$, for $x<-3$, the slope is positive.
This is represented in the slope function $y=g(x)$, where $g(x)$ is positive for $x<-3$.
> From A to B , the slope of the curve is negative. That is, from $x>-3$ and $x<2$, the slope is negative.
It can be seen that the slope function is negative from $-3<x<2$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-117.jpg?height=685&width=628&top_left_y=230&top_left_x=1004)
> At $x=-3$ and $x=2$, the slope of the cubic function is zero.
At these two $x$-values, the slope function is also zero.
$>$ For $x>2$, the slope of the cubic function is positive.
For $x>2$, the slope function reflects this since it is also positive for $x>2$.

The graph of the function $y=f(x)$ is shown.
Use the graph to write down the range of values of $x$ for which
(i) $f^{\prime}(x)>0$
(ii) $f^{\prime}(x)<0$
(iii) $f^{\prime}(x)=0$.
(i) $f^{\prime}(x)$ represents the slope of the curve at any point.
$\therefore f^{\prime}(x)>0$ for $-1<x<5$

(ii) $f^{\prime}(x)<0$ for $x<-1$ and $x>5$
(iii) $f^{\prime}(x)=0$ at $x=-1$ and at $x=5$

## Section 4.4 Maximum and minimum problems

In section 4.2, it was shown that differential calculus can be used in a very effective way to show that the maximum value of the given function is 10 , and that this occurs when $x=2$, as seen from the graph over.

In this section, we will show how differential calculus can also be used to maximise or minimise the value of any
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-121.jpg?height=296&width=416&top_left_y=1733&top_left_x=1216) function, such as $ C$ to represent cost, Area ( $A$ ) to represent area, or $V$ to represent volume.

The solutions of the more basic problems involving maxima and minima will consist of the following steps:

1. Draw a diagram, where necessary, and mark on it the given dimensions.
2. Write down an expression in one variable for the quantity to be maximised or minimised. If the quantity is in two variables, use the given information to express one of the variables in terms of the other so that the quantity may then be expressed in terms of one variable.
3. Differentiate the function and equate it to zero.
4. Solve this equation to find the value(s) of $x$ at which the maximum or minimum value occurs.
5. Substitute this value for $x$ in the given equation to find the maximum or minimum value.
6. Use the second derivative test (i.e. $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} y}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$ ) to determine if the value(s) for $x$ gives a maximum or minimum value of the function.

## Example 1

A factory produces $n$ items per hour.
The overhead costs, $C(n)=\left(400-16 n+\frac{n^{2}}{4}\right)$.
How many items should be produced to keep the overhead costs to a minimum?

$$
\begin{array}{ll}
C=\left(400-16 n+\frac{n^{2}}{4}\right) & \frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} C}{\mathrm{~d} n^{2}}=\frac{1}{2} \text { which is }>0 \\
\frac{\mathrm{~d} C}{\mathrm{~d} n}=-16+\frac{n}{2} & \\
\frac{\mathrm{~d} C}{\mathrm{~d} n}=0 & \Rightarrow-16+\frac{n}{2}=0 \\
& \Rightarrow n=32
\end{array}
$$

Since $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} C}{\mathrm{~d} n^{2}}>0, n=32$ is the number that should be produced to keep the overheads costs to a minimum.

## Example 2

A closed rectangular box is made of thin metal. The length of the box is three times its width. The volume of the box is $36 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ and its width is $x \mathrm{~cm}$.
Show that the surface area is $\left(6 x^{2}+\frac{96}{x}\right) \mathrm{cm}^{2}$.
Find the dimensions of the box with least surface area.
Volume $=36 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Rightarrow & 3 x^{2} h=36 \\
\Rightarrow & x^{2} h=12 \\
\Rightarrow & h=\frac{12}{x^{2}}
\end{aligned}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-122.jpg?height=197&width=388&top_left_y=1971&top_left_x=862)

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Surface area }=2(3 x h)+2(x h)+2\left(3 x^{2}\right) \\
&=6 x h+2 x h+6 x^{2} \\
&=8 x h+6 x^{2} \\
&=8 x\left(\frac{12}{x^{2}}\right)+6 x^{2}=\left(\frac{96}{x}+6 x^{2}\right) \mathrm{cm}^{2} \quad \ldots\left(h=\frac{12}{x^{2}}\right) \\
& \Rightarrow \text { Surface area }(S)=\left(6 x^{2}+\frac{96}{x}\right) \mathrm{cm}^{2} \\
& \frac{\mathrm{~d} S}{\mathrm{~d} x}=12 x-\frac{96}{x^{2}} \quad \ldots\left(\frac{96}{x}=96 x^{-1}\right) \\
& \frac{\mathrm{d} S}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0 \Rightarrow 12 x-\frac{96}{x^{2}}=0 \\
& \Rightarrow 12 x^{3}-96=0 \Rightarrow x^{3}=8 \Rightarrow x=2
\end{aligned}
$$

To investigate if this value of $x$ gives a maximum or a minimum value, we find $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} S}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{d} S}{\mathrm{~d} x}=12 x-\frac{96}{x^{2}} & =12 x-96 x^{-2} \\
\Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} S}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}} & =12+192 x^{-3} \\
& =12+\frac{192}{x^{3}}=12+\frac{192}{8} \text { when } x=2 \\
& =36>0 \Rightarrow \text { minimum value }
\end{aligned}
$$

When $x=2$, the dimensions of the box are:

$$
\text { length }=3 x=6 ; \text { breadth }=x=2 ; \text { height }=\frac{12}{x^{2}}=3
$$

$\therefore$ The dimensions are 6 cm by 2 cm by 3 cm .

## Example 3

A rectangle is formed by using the positive $x$-axis and $y$-axis and a point $\mathrm{B}(x, y)$ on the line $2 x+y=6$, as shown.
(i) Find an expression for the area, $A$ square units, of this rectangle in terms of $x$.
(ii) What is the domain set of values of $x$ ?
(iii) Use differentiation to find the maximum possible area of this rectangle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-123.jpg?height=539&width=347&top_left_y=1607&top_left_x=1194)
(i) Area $=$ length $\times$ breadth

$$
A=x(y)=x y
$$

We now express the area in terms of one variable.
Since $2 x+y=6 \Rightarrow y=6-2 x$
Area $(A)=x(6-2 x)=6 x-2 x^{2}$
(ii) Since the line intersects the $x$-axis at $(3,0) \Rightarrow 0<x<3$.
(iii) $A=6 x-2 x^{2}$
$\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} x}=6-4 x$
$\Rightarrow 6-4 x=0 \Rightarrow x=1.5$... for a maximum or minimum to occur, $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0$
$\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} A}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}=-4$, which is less than zero.
When $x=1.5$, a maximum occurs since $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} A}{\mathrm{~d} x^{2}}<0$.
Maximum area $=6(1.5)-2(1.5)^{2}=4.5$ square units.

## Example 4

A man wishes to get from a point A on one side of a river to a point C on the opposite side, as shown. The point C is 9 km from the point B which is 3 km directly across the river from A .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-124.jpg?height=265&width=588&top_left_y=1164&top_left_x=946)

The man can row at $4 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{hr}$ and walk at $5 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{hr}$. If the man rows from A to D , and walks from D to C , find $|\mathrm{BD}|$ such that the man gets from A to C in the least possible time.

Let $|\mathrm{BD}|=x \mathrm{~km}$
$\Rightarrow|\mathrm{DC}|=(9-x) \mathrm{km}$
and $|\mathrm{AD}|=\sqrt{x^{2}+9} \mathrm{~km}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-124.jpg?height=265&width=609&top_left_y=1612&top_left_x=933)

Time taken to go from A to $\mathrm{D}=\frac{\sqrt{x^{2}+9}}{4}$ hours ... (Time $\left.=\frac{\text { Distance }}{\text { Speed }}\right)$
Time taken to go from D to $\mathrm{C}=\frac{9-x}{5}$ hours
$\Rightarrow$ Total time, $T=\left(\frac{\sqrt{x^{2}+9}}{4}+\frac{9-x}{5}\right)$ hours

$$
\begin{aligned}
& =\frac{1}{4}\left(x^{2}+9\right)^{\frac{1}{2}}+\frac{1}{5}(9-x) \\
\frac{\mathrm{d} T}{\mathrm{~d} x} & =\frac{1}{4} \cdot \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{2 x}{\sqrt{x^{2}+9}}-\frac{1}{5} \\
& =\frac{x}{4 \sqrt{x^{2}+9}}-\frac{1}{5} \\
\frac{\mathrm{~d} T}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0 & \Rightarrow \frac{x}{4 \sqrt{x^{2}+9}}-\frac{1}{5}=0 \\
& \Rightarrow \frac{x}{4 \sqrt{x^{2}+9}}=\frac{1}{5} \\
& \Rightarrow \frac{x^{2}}{16\left(x^{2}+9\right)}=\frac{1}{25} \\
& \Rightarrow 25 x^{2}=16 x^{2}+144 \\
& \Rightarrow 9 x^{2}=144 \\
& \Rightarrow x^{2}=16 \Rightarrow x= \pm 4 \\
& \Rightarrow x=4 \mathrm{~km} \quad \ldots \text { disregard the negative answer } \\
& \Rightarrow|\mathrm{BD}|=4 \mathrm{~km}
\end{aligned}
$$

## Section 4.5 Rates of change

In our study of calculus so far, we have seen that $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$ represents the rate of change of $y$ with respect to $x$.
Similarly, if $V$ represents volume and $t$ represents time, then $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ is the rate of change of volume with respect to time.

It is clear that the process of differentiation may be used to tackle many kinds of problems involving rates of change.

One of the more important applications of the rate of change concerns the movement of a particle which starts at a fixed point O .
The displacement is defined as the change in position of the particle relative to O .
In this study of motion in a straight line, equations of the type $s=2 t^{2}-3 t+4$ occur frequently.
In this equation, $s$ represents the distance travelled (displacement) and $t$ represents time.
The rate of change of $s$ with respect to $t$ (i.e. velocity) is given by $\frac{\mathbf{d} \boldsymbol{s}}{\mathbf{d} \boldsymbol{t}}$.
Velocity may be positive, negative or zero.
If the velocity is positive, the particle is moving to the right.
If the velocity is negative, the direction of the motion is to the left.
A velocity of zero means the particle is instantaneously at rest.

The rate of change of velocity with respect to time is called the acceleration, $a$, of the object in motion.
$\therefore$ acceleration $(a)=\frac{\mathbf{d} \boldsymbol{v}}{\mathbf{d} t}$ or $\frac{\mathbf{d}^{2} \boldsymbol{s}}{\mathbf{d} \boldsymbol{t}^{2}}$.

If $s$ represents the distance travelled in time $t$, then
Speed and
(i) $\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ is the velocity of the object at any given time

Acceleration
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} s}{\mathrm{~d} t^{2}}$ represents the acceleration of the object.

Note: In the vast majority of the questions in the next exercise, 'velocity' and speed are interchangeable.

## Example 1

A body moves in a straight line so that its distance $s$, in metres, from a fixed point after $t$ secs is given by the equation

$$
s=10+27 t-t^{3}
$$

Find (i) the speed after 2 seconds
(ii) after how many seconds is the body at rest
(iii) the acceleration after 2 seconds.
(i) $s=10+27 t-t^{3}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { Speed }=\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t} & =27-3 t^{2} \\
& =27-3(2)^{2} \\
& =15 \text { metres/sec, after } 2 \text { seconds }
\end{aligned}
$$

(ii) If the body is at rest, the speed is zero.

$$
\begin{align*}
\frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}=0 & \Rightarrow 27-3 t^{2}=0 \\
& \Rightarrow 3 t^{2}=27 \Rightarrow t^{2}=9 \Rightarrow t=3 \tag{t>0}
\end{align*}
$$

$\therefore$ the body is at rest after 3 seconds
(iii) Acceleration is $\frac{\mathrm{d}^{2} s}{\mathrm{~d} t^{2}}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \frac{\mathrm{d} s}{\mathrm{~d} t}=27-3 t^{2} \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d}^{2} s}{\mathrm{~d} t^{2}}=-6 t \\
& \Rightarrow \text { acceleration }=-12 \text { metres } / \mathrm{sec}^{2}, \text { when } t=2
\end{aligned}
$$

Note: metres $/ \sec ^{2}$ represents "metres per second per second".

## Example 2

A marketing director of a company found that the revenue, $ R$, from selling a fixed number of produced items at $ P$ each is given by the formula

$$
R=30 P-2 P^{2}
$$

(i) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}$ and explain what this means.
(ii) Calculate $\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}$ when $P=10$.
(iii) For what selling prices is revenue rising?
(i) $R=30 P-2 P^{2}$
$\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}=30-4 P$
$\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}$ is the rate at which the revenue is changing relative to the price, $P$.
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}=30-4 P=30-4(10)=-10$, when $P=10$
(iii) The revenue is rising when $\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}$ is positive.

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\mathrm{d} R}{\mathrm{~d} P}>0 \text { when } & 30-4 P>0 \\
30 & >4 P \\
4 P & <30 \\
P & <7.5
\end{aligned}
$$

For $P< 7.50$, the revenue increases as $P$ increases.

Note: In general, the phrase "the rate of change of ..." is taken to mean the rate of change with respect to time.
However, each question must be read carefully to identify the relevant variables and rates.

## Exercise 4.5

## Section 4.6 Related rates of change

In the previous section, we dealt with the use of the derivative as a measure of the rate of change of a function with respect to the variable.
If $A=\pi r^{2}$ represents the area of a circle of radius $r$, then $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}$ is the rate of change of the area with respect to $r$.

However, in practical cases, most rates of change are measured with respect to time. For example, if $V$ represents volume,
$\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ is the rate of change of volume with respect to time.
In many situations, we are given one rate of change and asked to find another rate of change. In these situations, the Chain Rule is particularly useful when finding the required rate of change.
The Chain Rule states that $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} u} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} u}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
This can be extended to $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} \bullet} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} \bullet}{\mathrm{~d} x}$, where $\bullet$ stands for any variable.
For example, $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}=\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathbf{d} t} \cdot \frac{\mathbf{d} t}{\mathrm{~d} r}$

If we are given that $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}=8$, then $\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} A}=\frac{1}{8}$.
Similarly, if $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}=6 \pi$, then $\frac{\mathrm{d} t}{\mathrm{~d} V}=\frac{1}{6 \pi}$.

$$
\frac{\mathrm{d} t}{\mathrm{~d} r}=\frac{1}{\frac{\mathrm{~d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}}
$$

The method of solving problems involving 'related rates' is illustrated in the following examples.

## Example 1

The radius of a circle is increasing at the rate of $2 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$.
Find the rate at which the area is increasing when the radius is 3 cm .
We are given $\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}=2$ and we require $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}$.

$$
\begin{array}{rl|r}
\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t} & =\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r} \\
& =2.2 \pi r \\
& =2.2 \pi(3) \quad \ldots r=3 \\
& =12 \pi & \begin{array}{r}
\text { To find } \frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}, \text { we us } \\
\text { formula } A=\pi r^{2}
\end{array} \\
\therefore \quad \frac{\mathrm{~d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t} & =12 \pi \mathrm{~cm}^{2} / \mathrm{sec}
\end{array} \quad \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}=2 \pi r
$$

When solving problems with related rates of change, these steps should prove useful.

1. Write down the rate you require, e.g. $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}$.
2. Write down the rate you are given, e.g. $\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}$.
3. Use the Chain Rule to link these rates: $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{?} \cdot \frac{?}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}$
4. Find an equation connecting the variables of the missing rate, i.e. $A=\pi r^{2}$.

## Example 2

The volume, $V \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, of water in a container is given by the expression $V=12 h^{2}$, where $h \mathrm{~cm}$ is the depth of the water.
Water is flowing into the container at a steady rate of $90 \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{sec}$.
Find the rate, in $\mathrm{cm} / \mathrm{sec}$, at which the depth of the water is increasing when $h=3$.

We require $\frac{\mathrm{d} h}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ and we are given $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}=90$.
Now, $\frac{\mathrm{d} h}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} h}{\mathrm{~d} V}$
We need to find $\frac{\mathrm{d} h}{\mathrm{~d} V}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& V=12 h^{2} \text { (given) } \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} h}=24 h \Rightarrow \frac{\mathrm{~d} h}{\mathrm{~d} V}=\frac{1}{24 h} \\
& \frac{\mathrm{~d} h}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t} \cdot \frac{\mathrm{~d} h}{\mathrm{~d} V} \\
& \quad=90 \times \frac{1}{24 h}=\frac{90}{24(3)}=\frac{90}{72}=\frac{5}{4} \text { when } h=3 \\
& \therefore \quad \frac{\mathrm{~d} h}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{5}{4} \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}
\end{aligned}
$$

## Exercise 4.6

1. In each of the following, fill in the missing rate:
(i) $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r} \cdot-$
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} r}=\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}$. -
(iii) $\frac{\mathrm{d} M}{\mathrm{~d} t}=\frac{\mathrm{d} M}{\mathrm{~d} s}$.
2. In each of the following, find the indicated rate.
(i) $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}=8, \frac{\mathrm{~d} A}{\mathrm{~d} r}=4, \frac{\mathrm{~d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}=$ ?
(ii) $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}=8, \frac{\mathrm{~d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}=2, \frac{\mathrm{~d} V}{\mathrm{~d} r}=?$
3. If $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}=10$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d} x}{\mathrm{~d} t}=2$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} t}$.
4. If $A=\pi r^{2}$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}=1$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} A}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ when $r=5$.
5. The radius of a circle is increasing at the rate of $3 \mathrm{~cm} /$ second.

Find the rate of increase of the area when $r=9 \mathrm{~cm}$.
6. The side, $x \mathrm{~cm}$, of a square is increasing at the rate of $5 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$.

Find the rate of increase of the area when the side is 10 cm in length.
7. If $M=(2 p+3)^{4}$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} M}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ when $p=1$, given that $\frac{\mathrm{d} p}{\mathrm{~d} t}=2$.
8. If $V=\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}$ and $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} t}=6 \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{sec}$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} r}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ when $r=3 \mathrm{~cm}$.
9. The volume of a sphere is increasing at the rate of $24 \pi \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{sec}$. Find the rate of increase of the radius when it is 6 cm in length.
10. The perimeter of a rectangle has a constant value of 40 cm . One side, of length $x \mathrm{~cm}$, is increasing at the rate of $0.5 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$.
(i) Find, in terms of $x$, an expression for the area of the rectangle.
(ii) Find the rate at which the area is increasing when $x=3 \mathrm{~cm}$.
11. The path of a football is given by the equation $y=x-\frac{x^{2}}{40}, x \geqslant 0$. If $\frac{\mathrm{d} x}{\mathrm{~d} t}=10 \sqrt{2}$ for all $t$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ when $x=10$.
12. A pump is inflating a spherical balloon.

The radius of the balloon is increasing at a rate of $1 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$ when the length of the radius is 2 metres.
(i) Find the rate at which the pump is working (i.e. the rate of change of the volume).
(ii) If the air continues to be pumped at this rate, find the rate of change of the radius when it is 5 metres in length.
(iii) At what rate is the surface area of the balloon increasing when $r=5 \mathrm{~m}$ ?
13. A 10 m ladder leans against a vertical wall. The top of the ladder is at a height of 8 m up the wall and is slipping down the wall at a rate of $1 \mathrm{~m} / \mathrm{sec}$.
At what rate is the foot of the ladder sliding along the ground when the top of the ladder is 8 m up the wall?
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-135.jpg?height=353&width=350&top_left_y=1193&top_left_x=1282)
14. A circular cylinder has a radius of $r \mathrm{~cm}$, height $4 r \mathrm{~cm}$ and a volume $V \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$.
Express $V$ in terms of $r$, and hence find an expression for $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} r}$.
The radius is increasing at the rate of $0.5 \mathrm{~cm} / \mathrm{sec}$ when the radius is 6 cm .
Find the rate at which the volume is increasing at this instant.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-135.jpg?height=310&width=221&top_left_y=1627&top_left_x=1351)
15. A circle has radius $r \mathrm{~cm}$, circumference $C \mathrm{~cm}$ and area $A \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$.

Show that $\frac{\mathrm{d} C}{\mathrm{~d} A}=\frac{1}{r}$.
The area of a circle is increasing at the rate of $2 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} / \mathrm{sec}$.
Find the rate of increase of the circumference when the radius is 3 cm .

## Revision Exercise 4 (Core)

1. Find the equation of the tangent to the curve $y=x^{2}-\frac{9}{x}$ at the point where $x=3$.
2. Find the stationary points of the curve $y=x^{3}-12 x+5$ and determine if each point is a maximum or a minimum.
3. The curve $f(x)=x^{3}-b x^{2}-9 x+7$ has a stationary point when $x=-1$.

Find the value of $b$.
4. Find the range of values of $x$ for which the function $f(x)=x^{3}+3 x^{2}-9 x$ is decreasing.
5. The point $P(x, y)$ lies on the curve with equation $y=6 x^{2}-x^{3}$.
(i) Find the value of $x$ for which the gradient of the tangent at $P$ is 12 .
(ii) Hence, find the equation of the tangent at $P$.
6. A particle moves in a straight line so that, after a time of $t$ seconds, its distance $s$ metres from a fixed point O is given by $s=2 t^{3}-24 t$.
(i) Find the speed of the particle after 4 seconds.
(ii) After how many seconds is the particle at rest?
7. The equation of a curve is $y=x \sin 2 x$.

Find the slope of the curve at the point where $x=\frac{\pi}{3}$.
8. For $x+y=100$, prove that product $P=x y$ is a maximum when $x=y$, and find the maximum value of $P$.
9. The number of salmon swimming upstream in a river in order to spawn is approximated by $s(x)=-x^{3}+3 x^{2}+360 x+5000$, with $x$ representing the temperature of the water in degrees ( ${ }^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ ). (This function is valid only if $6 \leqslant x \leqslant 20$.)
Find the water temperature that results in the maximum number of salmon swimming upstream.
10. The volume of a cube, of edge $x \mathrm{~cm}$, is increasing at the rate of $12 \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{second}$. Find the rate of increase of the length of the edge of the cube when the volume of the cube is $125 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$.
11. The equation of a curve is $y=x+\frac{4}{x}$.

Show that $(2,4)$ is a minimum turning point and find the coordinates of the maximum turning point.
Find also the set of values of $x>0$ at which the curve is increasing.
12. The curve of $f(x)=a x^{2}+b x+c$ passes through the point $(2,24)$ and the gradient of the curve at this point is 22 .
The value of $f^{\prime \prime}(x)$ is 6 .
(i) Find the values of $a, b$ and $c$.
(ii) Find the coordinates of the points where the curve crosses the $x$-axis.
(iii) Find the coordinates of the minimum turning point.
13. Gas is escaping from a spherical balloon at the rate of $10 \pi \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ per minute. How fast is the radius decreasing when the radius is 5 cm ?
14. A projectile is fired vertically upwards from the ground and the distance, $s$ metres, above the ground is given by $s=196 t-4.9 t^{2}$, where $t$ seconds is the time measured from the time of projection.
Find the time taken to reach the maximum height and the maximum height attained by the projectile.
15. The graph of a cubic function $y=f(x)$ is shown on the right.

One of the four diagrams $\mathrm{A}, \mathrm{B}, \mathrm{C}, \mathrm{D}$ below shows the graph of the derivative of $f$.

State which one it is, and justify your answer.

## Revision Exercise 4 (Advanced)

1. Find the equation of the tangent to the curve $f(x)=\sqrt{x^{2}-3}$ at the point $(2,1)$.
2. Find the coordinates of the two points on the curve $y=x^{2}+\ln x$ where the slope of the tangent is 3 .
3. A spherical hot-air balloon is being blown up so that its volume increases at a constant rate of $2 \mathrm{~m}^{3}$ per minute.
(i) Find the rate of increase of the radius when $r=2.5 \mathrm{~m}$.
(ii) Find the rate of increase of its surface area when $r=2.5 \mathrm{~m}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-138.jpg?height=305&width=291&top_left_y=173&top_left_x=1337)
4. The diagram shows a sketch of the curve with equation $y=x^{3}-6 x^{2}+8 x$.
(a) Find the coordinates of the points on the curve where the gradient of the tangent is -1 .
(b) The line $y=4-x$ is a tangent to this curve at a point A . Find the coordinates of A .
(c) Draw a rough sketch of the graph of $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-138.jpg?height=482&width=512&top_left_y=532&top_left_x=1116)
5. The diagram shows a rectangular cake-box, with no top, which is made from thin card. The volume of the box is $500 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$. The base of the box is a square with sides of length $x \mathrm{~cm}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-138.jpg?height=183&width=376&top_left_y=1054&top_left_x=1255)
(i) Show that the area, $A \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$, of card used to make the box is given by $A=x^{2}+\frac{2000}{x}$.
(ii) Find the minimum area of card used.
6. The cost of manufacturing an object depends on the time, $t$ hours, spent working on it. The cost $ C$ can be modelled by the equation $ C=\frac{16}{t^{3}}+\frac{3 t^{2}}{4}$.
(i) Find the rate of change of the cost when $t=4$ hours.
(ii) Find the minimum cost of manufacturing the object.
7. If $y=x \mathrm{e}^{x}$, find $\frac{\mathrm{d} y}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.

Hence, find the coordinates of the turning point of the curve $y=x \mathrm{e}^{x}$ and determine if it is a maximum or a minimum turning point.

9. The curve $y=x^{3}+a x^{2}+b x+c$ passes through the point $(1,1)$ and has turning points at $x=-1$ and $x=3$.

It has a semicircular top on a rectangular base, as shown.

Here are two statements about $f(x)$ :
(1) $f(x)<0$ for $s<x<t$
(2) $f^{\prime}(x) \leqslant 0$ for $x<q$

Which of the following is true?
A Neither statement is correct.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-139.jpg?height=404&width=418&top_left_y=1712&top_left_x=1210)

B Only statement (1) is correct.
C Only statement (2) is correct.
D Both statements are correct.
13. The equation of a curve is $f(x)=x^{3}+3 k x^{2}+32, k \in R$.

Find the coordinates of the two turning points of the curve, giving the coordinates of one of these points in terms of $k$.
Hence, find the value of $k$ for which the equation $f(x)=0$ has three real roots, two of which are equal.
14. A house is to be connected to a mains gas supply at S , a distance of 4 m horizontally away and 3 m below the surface.
It costs $ 25$ per metre to lay the pipeline underground and $ 10$ per metre along the ground.

## Revision Exercise 4 (Extended Response Questions)

1. This diagram shows a right circular cylindrical metal rod which is expanding as it is heated. After $t$ seconds, the radius of the rod is $x \mathrm{~cm}$ and the length of the rod is $5 x \mathrm{~cm}$.

The cross-sectional area of the rod is increasing at the constant rate of $0.032 \mathrm{~cm}^{2} / \mathrm{sec}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-140.jpg?height=237&width=340&top_left_y=1541&top_left_x=1294)
(a) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} x}{\mathrm{~d} t}$ when the radius of the rod is 2 cm , giving your answer to 3 significant figures.
(b) Find the rate of increase of the volume of the rod when $x=2$.
2. A rectangular block is such that the sides of its base are of length $x \mathrm{~cm}$ and $3 x \mathrm{~cm}$. The sum of the lengths of all its edges is 20 cm .
(a) Find an expression, in terms of $x$, for the height of the block.
(b) Show that the volume, $V \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$, is given by $V=15 x^{2}-12 x^{3}$.
(c) What values of $x$ should be considered, i.e. find the domain of the function with the rule $V=15 x^{2}-12 x^{3}$ ?
(d) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
(e) Find $\left\{x: \frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0\right\}$ and hence find the maximum volume possible.
3. The height, $h$ metres, above level ground of a ball thrown vertically upwards is given by $h=2+40 t-5 t^{2}$, where $t$ is measured in seconds and $t>0$.
(i) Find the rate of change of the height when
(a) $t=2$
(b) $t=2.5$
(ii) Find when the rate of change of height is zero. Describe the motion of the ball near this value of $t$.
(iii) Find the height of the ball when the rate of change of height is zero.
(iv) Find the rate of change of the height when $t=6$ and interpret the result.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-141.jpg?height=534&width=286&top_left_y=518&top_left_x=1347)
(v) If the rate of change of height measures the speed of the ball, find the speed with which the ball leaves the hand.
(vi) By finding the value of $t$, correct to 2 places of decimals, estimate the speed of the ball as it hits the ground.
4. A right circular cone lies inside a sphere of radius 1 m , as shown. The centre of the sphere, O , lies $x \mathrm{~m}$ from the base of the cone.
(a) The volume of a cone is given by the formula $V=\frac{1}{3} \pi r^{2} h$.
Find: (i) $r$ in terms of $x$
(ii) $h$ in terms of $x$
(b) Show that $V=\frac{\pi}{3}\left(1+x-x^{2}-x^{3}\right)$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-141.jpg?height=423&width=507&top_left_y=1288&top_left_x=1128)
(c) State a suitable domain for the function with rule $V=\frac{\pi}{3}\left(1+x-x^{2}-x^{3}\right)$.
(d) (i) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} x}$.
(ii) Find $\left\{x: \frac{\mathrm{d} V}{\mathrm{~d} x}=0\right\}$.
(iii) State the maximum possible volume of the cone.
5. A boatman at the point A is 6 km from the nearest point C on a straight shoreline. He wishes to get to the point $\mathrm{B}, 10 \mathrm{~km}$ from C , where $|\angle \mathrm{ACB}|=90^{\circ}$.
He can row the boat at $5 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{hr}$ and travel along the shoreline at $13 \mathrm{~km} / \mathrm{hr}$. He rows to the point P where
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-142.jpg?height=338&width=646&top_left_y=173&top_left_x=983) $|\mathrm{PC}|=x \mathrm{~km}$ and then travels by road along the shoreline to B .
(i) Express $|\mathrm{AP}|$ and $|\mathrm{PB}|$ in terms of $x$.
(ii) Find $x$ if the time taken to go from A to B is a minimum.
(iii) Calculate this minimum time, correct to the nearest minute.
6. The population density, $P$, of a city depends on the radius chosen from the centre of the city.
For a particular city, $P=10+40 r-20 r^{2}$, where $P$ is measured in thousands and $r$ is measured in kilometres from the centre of the city.
(a) What is the population density at the centre of the city?
(b) What are the possible values of $r$ ? (i.e. find the domain of values of $r$.)
(c) Sketch a graph of $P$ against $r$.
(d) Find $\frac{\mathrm{d} P}{\mathrm{~d} r}$.
(e) Evaluate $\frac{\mathrm{d} P}{\mathrm{~d} r}$ at $r=0.5,1$ and 2 .
(f) Find the value of $r$ (the distance from the centre of the city), where the population density is greatest. Hence, find the population density for this value of $r$.
7. An open cylindrical tank of water has a hole near the bottom. The radius of the tank is 52 cm . The hole is a circle of radius 1 cm . The water level gradually drops as water escapes through the hole.

Over a certain 20 -minute period, the height of the surface of the water is given by the formula

$$
h=\left(10-\frac{t}{200}\right)^{2}
$$

where $h$ is the height of the surface of the water in cm , as measured from the centre of the hole, and $t$ is the time
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-142.jpg?height=468&width=336&top_left_y=1480&top_left_x=1296) in seconds from a particular instant $t=0$.
(a) What is the height of the surface at time $t=0$ ?
(b) After how many seconds will the height of the surface be 64 cm ?
(c) Find the rate at which the volume of water in the tank is decreasing at the instant when the height is 64 cm .
Give your answer correct to the nearest $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$ per second.
(d) The rate at which the volume of water in the tank is decreasing is equal to the speed of the water coming out of the hole, multiplied by the area of the hole.
Find the speed at which the water is coming out of the hole at the instant when the height is 64 cm .
(e) Show that, as $t$ varies, the speed of the water coming out of the hole is a constant multiple of $\sqrt{h}$.
(f) The speed, in centimetres per second, of water coming out of a hole like this is known to be given by the formula

$$
v=c \sqrt{1962 h}
$$

where $c$ is a constant that depends on certain features of the hole.
Find, correct to one decimal place, the value of $c$ for this hole.
8. A container in the shape of a right circular cone of height 10 cm and base radius 1 cm is used to catch water leaking from a tap.
The tap is leaking at a rate of $0.1 \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{sec}$.
(a) Write an equation for the volume of water in the cone in terms of $r$ and $h$.
(b) Using similar triangles, find a relationship between $r$ and $h$.
(c) Express the volume in terms of $h$.
(d) Write the rate $0.1 \mathrm{~cm}^{3} / \mathrm{sec}$ as a differential.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-143.jpg?height=453&width=465&top_left_y=740&top_left_x=1168)
(e) Using the chain rule, find the rate at which the water is rising when the water is half-way up the cone.
(f) Use the result in (e) above to find the rate at which the surface area is increasing at this point.
9. A company uses waterproof paper to make disposable conical drinking cups. To make each cup, a sector AOB is cut from a circular piece of paper of radius 9 cm . The edges AO and OB are then joined to form the cup, as shown.

The radius of the rim of the cup is $r$, and the height of the cup is $h$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-143.jpg?height=367&width=706&top_left_y=1646&top_left_x=609)
(a) By expressing $r^{2}$ in terms of $h$, show that the capacity of the cup, in $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$, is given by the formula

$$
V=\frac{\pi}{3} h\left(81-h^{2}\right)
$$

(b) There are two positive values of $h$ for which the capacity of the cup is $\frac{154 \pi}{3}$.

One of these values is an integer.
Find the two values.
Give the non-integer value correct to two decimal places.
(c) Find the maximum possible volume of the cup, correct to the nearest $\mathrm{cm}^{3}$.
(d) Complete the table below to show the radius, height, and capacity of each of the cups involved in parts (b) and (c) above.
In each case, give the radius and height correct to two decimal places.

|  | cups in part (b) |  | cup in part (c) |
| :--- | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| radius $(\boldsymbol{r})$ |  |  |  |
| height $(\boldsymbol{h})$ |  |  |  |
| capacity $(\boldsymbol{V})$ | $\frac{154 \pi}{3} \approx 161 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ | $\frac{154 \pi}{3} \approx 161 \mathrm{~cm}^{3}$ |  |

(e) In practice, which one of the three cups above is the most reasonable shape for a conical cup?
Give a reason for your answer.
(f) For the cup you have chosen in part (e), find the measure of the angle AOB that must be cut from the circular disc in order to make the cup.
Give your answer in degrees, correct to the nearest degree.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-144.jpg?height=441&width=402&top_left_y=1062&top_left_x=1065)
10. A rare species of bird lives only on a remote island. A mathematical model predicts that the bird population, $P$, is given by

$$
P=150+300 e^{-0.05 t}
$$

where $t$ is the number of years after observations began.
(a) According to the model, how many birds were there when observations began?
(b) According to the model, what will be the rate of change in the bird population ten years after observations began?
(c) What does the model predict will be the limiting value (i.e. as $t$ approaches infinity) of the bird population?
(d) The species will become eligible for inclusion in the WWF endangered species list when the population falls below 200 . When does the model predict that this will occur?

## Financial Maths

## Key words

(i) Compound Interest
(ii) Future Value
(iii) Present Value
(iv) Pensions
(v) Annuity
(vi) Mortgage repayments
(vii) Instalment savings, etc.

## Section 5.1 Compound interest

When the interest earned by a sum of money on deposit in a bank is not withdrawn, it is added to the principal for the second year.

The interest grows year by year and is said to be compounded.

At a rate of 5\% per annum, the increase in the interest earned by $ 10000$ can be seen year-onyear from the table.

| Year | Principal | Interest |
| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| One | $ 10000$ | $ 500$ |
| Two | $ 10500$ | $ 525$ |
| Three | $ 11025$ | $ 551$ |
| Four | $ 11576$ | $ 579$ |
| Five | $ 12155$ | $ 608$ |
|  | $ 12763$ |  |

If the interest is withdrawn and not reinvested each year, a total of $5 \times  500= 2500$ is earned.
Compounding the interest, a total of $ 2763$ is earned.
Generally, if $i$ (per cent, expressed as a decimal) is the interest rate, then $i \times \mathrm{P}$ is the interest earned, where $ \mathrm{P}$ is the principal or investment at the beginning of the year.
At the start of the second year, there is $\mathrm{P}+i \mathrm{P}=\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+\boldsymbol{i})^{\mathbf{1}}$ on deposit in the bank.
The change in the principal, year-by-year, can be seen from the following table:

| Year (end) | Principal | Interest |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
|  | P | $i \times \mathrm{P}$ |
| one | $\mathbf{P}+i \mathbf{P}=\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+i)^{\mathbf{1}}$ | $i \times \mathrm{P}(1+i)$ |
| two | $\mathrm{P}(1+i)+i \mathrm{P}(1+i)=\mathrm{P}(1+i)(1+i)=\mathbf{P}(1+i)^{2}$ | $i \times \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{2}$ |
| three | $\mathbf{P}(1+i)^{2}+i \mathbf{P}(1+i)^{2}=\mathbf{P}(1+i)^{2}(1+i)=\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+i)^{\mathbf{3}}$ |  |
| year $t$ | $=\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+\boldsymbol{i})^{\boldsymbol{t}}$ |  |

At the end of year $t$, there is $\boldsymbol{} \mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+\boldsymbol{i})^{\boldsymbol{t}}$ on deposit.
For example, $ 10000$, invested for 5 years at $5 \%$ compounded annually, amounts to $ 10000(1+0.05)^{5}= 12,763 . \therefore \quad$ the interest gained $= 2763$.

APR ("Annual Percentage Rate") is the "true" annual rate of interest charged for borrowing money.

AER ("Annual Equivalent Rate") is the rate of interest paid on investments.

## 1. Final value

If the interest is fixed for a period of time $t$, then the principal $ \mathrm{P}$ added to the compound interest is called the final value of P.

The final value of a sum of money $ \mathrm{P}$, invested now at $i \%$ for $t$ years, is

$$
\begin{gathered}
\text { Final value }(\mathbf{F})= \mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+\boldsymbol{i})^{\boldsymbol{t}} \\
\text { Interest earned }= \mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}+\boldsymbol{i})^{\boldsymbol{t}}- \mathbf{P}
\end{gathered}
$$

## Example 1

Find the final value of $ 5000$ invested at $4 \%$ (AER) per annum, compounded annually, for 6 years. Find also the interest earned over the period.

Future value $= \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{t}= 5000(1+0.04)^{6}= 6326.60$
Interest earned $= 6326.60- 5000= 1326.60$

## Example 2

An investment bond offers a return of $15 \%$ if invested for 4 years.
Calculate the AER (annual equivalent rate) for this bond, as a percentage, correct to two places of decimals.

A return of $15 \%$ after 4 years means that the amount (future value) is 1.15 times the sum invested, P.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \therefore \quad  P(1+i)^{4}= P(1.15) \\
& \therefore \quad(1+i)^{4}=1.15 \\
& (1+i)=1.15^{\frac{1}{4}}=1.03555 \ldots \text { taking the fourth root of both sides } \\
& \begin{aligned}
\therefore \quad i(\text { annual equivalent rate }) & =.03555=3.555 \% \\
& =3.56 \%
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}
$$

It is now common practice to have interest "added monthly" to a savings account. In this case a monthly interest rate is applied that is equivalent to the annual rate.

If $r$ is the monthly rate and $i=5 \%$ is the annual equivalent rate (AER), then for 12 payments

$$
\begin{aligned}
& (1+r)^{12}=(1+i)=(1+0.05)=1.05 \\
& \therefore \quad(1+r)=(1.05)^{\frac{1}{12}}=1.004074 \\
& \therefore \quad r=1.004074-1=0.004074=0.4074 \%
\end{aligned}
$$

$\therefore 5 \%$ per year is equivalent to $0.4074 \%$ per month.
Note: $i$ is always expressed as a decimal, e.g. $5 \%=0.05$

To calculate monthly interest rates, $r \%$ :

$$
(1+r)^{12}=(1+i) \quad r \% \text { per month, } i \% \text { per year }
$$

## Example 3

$ 5000$ is invested at $4 \% \mathrm{AER}$. If the interest is added monthly, find the final value of this investment after (i) $3 \frac{1}{2}$ years (ii) 5 years 2 months.

The monthly effective rate $r$ is found by solving the equation $(1+r)^{12}=1.04$.

$$
\begin{array}{ll}
\therefore & (1+r)=1.04^{\frac{1}{12}}=1.003274 \\
\therefore & r=0.003274=0.3274 \% \text { per month. }
\end{array}
$$

The final value after
(i) $3 \frac{1}{2}$ years $=3 \frac{1}{2} \times 12=42$ months

$$
\Rightarrow F=5000(1.003274)^{42}= 5735.77
$$

(ii) 5 years 2 months $=62$ months

$$
\Rightarrow F=5000(1.003274)^{62}= 6123.26 .
$$

## 2. Present value

To find the present value of $ 10000$ due in three years time, we must calculate what sum of money, invested at $i \% ~($ e.g. $5 \%=0.05)$ compounded annually, would have a final value of $ 10000$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \text { Final value }=\text { Present value } \times(1+i)^{t} \\
& \Rightarrow \text { Present value }=\frac{\text { Future value }}{(1+i)^{t}} \\
& \Rightarrow \text { Present value }=\frac{10000}{(1.05)^{3}}= 8638.38
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
\text { Present value }=\frac{\text { Final value }}{(1+i)^{t}}
$$

Therefore, at an annual equivalent rate of $5 \%$, the present value of $ 10000$ (in 3 years time) is 8638.38.

## Example 4

The local GAA club runs a draw.
You win first prize and you are offered
(a) $ 15000$ now or
(b) $ 18000$ in four years time.

When calculating present value, the rate $i \%$ is often referred to as the "discount rate".

Which prize should you choose to have the greatest value? Assume a discount rate of $4 \%$.

The present value of $ 18000$ based on a discount rate of $4 \%$ is

$$
\text { Present value }=\frac{\text { Final value }}{(1+i)^{t}}=\frac{18000}{(1.04)^{3}}= 15386.48
$$

Therefore, the best value prize, based on a discount rate of $4 \%$, is option (b) $ 18000$ in four years time.

## Summary

| Final value: $\mathrm{F}=\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{t}$ | Present value: $\mathrm{P}=\frac{\mathrm{F}}{(1+i)^{t}}$ |
| :--- | :--- |

## Example 5

In how many years would $ 5000$ increase in value to $ 6500$ if invested at an AER of $3.5 \%$ ?

$$
\begin{gathered}
\mathrm{F}=\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{t} \\
\therefore \quad  6500= 5000(1.035)^{t}
\end{gathered}
$$

Method 1 (Logs)(See chapter 12 book 4)
$\Rightarrow \quad(1.035)^{t}=\frac{ 6500}{ 5000}=1.3$
$\Rightarrow \boldsymbol{\operatorname { l n }}(1.035)^{t}=\boldsymbol{\operatorname { l n }} 1.3 \ldots$ taking the $\log$ of both sides
$\Rightarrow \quad t \ln (1.035)=\ln 1.3 \ldots \ln \mathrm{~A}^{n}=n \ln \mathrm{~A}$
$\Rightarrow t=\frac{\ln 1.3}{\ln (1.035)}=7.63$ years

Method 2 (Calculator)
$\Rightarrow \quad(1.035)^{t}=1.3$
$\Rightarrow t=\log _{1.035}(1.3)=7.63 \ldots$
using the logal key on the calculator.

4. What monthly rate of interest, correct to 2 places of decimals, is equivalent to an annual rate of (i) $6 \% \quad$ (ii) $2.5 \% \quad$ (iii) $4 \%$ ?

Find the AER that applied to his investment.

| :--- | :---: | :---: |
| One | $ 15000$ |  |

| Three |  |  |

If the bank offers an AER of $4 \%$, find, correct to four significant figures, the equivalent half-yearly rate.

Find the value of the investment after
(i) 5 years 3 months
(ii) 8 years 2 months
(iii) 10 years 6 months.

What is the value of her investment at the end of this period?

## Section 5.2 Depreciation

In the previous section, money lodged into a savings account appreciated in value.
The final value was greater than the present value.
Depreciation occurs when the final value of an asset is less than the present value.
Cars, computers and household appliances generally depreciate in value over time.
Houses in Ireland appreciated in value up to 2007 but have since greatly depreciated in value relative to this "peak" value. Two types of depreciation can be considered.

1. Straight line depreciation occurs when the value of an object reduces by a constant amount each year.
For example, take a car costing $ 20000$ that loses $10 \%$ of its original value each year.
This car loses 2000 in value each year and so the car has no value after 10 years.
2. Reducing balance depreciation occurs when the value of an object reduces by a fixed percentage of its value each year.
Consider a car costing 20000 that loses $10 \%$ of its value each year on a reducing balance.
The value of the car after 10 years $= 20000(1-0.1)^{10}= 6973.57$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-150.jpg?height=696&width=989&top_left_y=1446&top_left_x=451)
(Note: Our course emphasises the reducing balance method for calculating depreciation.)

Similar to compound interest when $\mathrm{F}=\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{t}$, the final value of an item based on a reducing balance is given by the formula $\mathbf{F}=\mathbf{P}(\mathbf{1}-\boldsymbol{i})^{\boldsymbol{t}}$.

```
F = final value
Depreciation: F= P(1-i)t}\quadi=\mathrm{ the percentage depreciation of P per year
    t= number of years
    P = initial value
```


## Example 1

A company buys a new machine priced at $ 35000$.
The machine depreciates by $20 \%$ on a reducing balance basis each year.
(i) What will the value of the machine be in 4 years time?
(ii) By how much has the machine depreciated in value during this time?
(i) Final value: $\mathrm{F}=\mathrm{P}(1-i)^{t}= 35000 \times(1-0.2)^{4}$ $= 35000 \times(0.8)^{4}= 14336$.
(ii) Depreciation $= 35000- 14336= 20664$.

## Example 2

A garage has a petrol stock of 100000 litres.
If the manager estimates (a) that he will sell 4000 litres a day
(b) that he will sell $5 \%$ of his stock per day, calculate the difference in his estimates after 20 days.

After 20 days: $4000 \times 20=80000$ litres sold

$$
\Rightarrow 100000-80000=20000 \text { litres left. }
$$

After 20 days the future (depleted) stock, $\mathrm{F}=\mathrm{P}(1-i)^{t}$

$$
=100000(1-0.05)^{20}=35849 \text { litres }
$$

Therefore the difference in his estimates is 15849 litres.

## Exercise 5.2

(In the following exercise, depreciation is used on a reducing balance basis unless otherwise stated.)

1. How much will a car, costing $ 30000$, be worth in

(ii) ten years time based on a depreciation of $15 \%$ per annum?

Find the value of the car after
(i) 3 years
(ii) 6 years.

10. An air-conditioning system cost $ 8000$. A straight line depreciation and a reducing balance curve for this system are shown below.

(i) Using the graph, estimate the rate of depreciation.
(Let the value after 20 years be 1.)
(ii) Explain why the reducing balance curve can never have a zero value.

## Section 5.3 Instalment savings (annuities)

## 1. Instalment saving

If a constant amount of money is saved each month for 3 years in total, then each instalment earns different (compound) interest, since it is invested for a different length of time. The first instalment is in the bank for 3 years = 36 months ; the last instalment is in the bank for only one month.
Let $ \mathrm{P}$ be invested at the beginning of each month for 3 years at a monthly rate of $i \%$.
The first payment has a value of $\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{36}$ after 36 months.
The second payment has a value of $\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{35}$ after 35 months, etc $\ldots$
The total value of the investment is the sum of these 36 individual amounts.
This sum is calculated as follows:

$$
\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{36}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{35}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{34}+\ldots \ldots . \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{2}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{1}
$$

If we reverse the order, we get

$$
\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{1}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{2}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{3} \ldots \ldots+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{35}+\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{36}
$$

which is the sum of the geometric series where
the first term, the common ratio, the number of terms,

$$
\left.\begin{array}{rl}
a & =\mathrm{P}(1+i)^{1} \\
r & =(1+i) \\
n & =36
\end{array}\right\}
$$

Using the formula for the sum of $n$ terms of a geometric sequence, $S_{n}=\frac{a\left(1-r^{n}\right)}{(1-r)}$, we get

$$
\begin{aligned}
\text { final value }=\frac{\mathrm{P}(1+i)\left[1-(1+i)^{36}\right]}{1-(1+i)}= & \frac{\mathrm{P}(1+i)\left[1-(1+i)^{36}\right]}{-i} \\
& =\frac{\mathrm{P}(1+i)\left[(1+i)^{36}-1\right]}{i}
\end{aligned}
$$

In general, the Final value, $\mathbf{F}$, of instalment savings over $\boldsymbol{t}$ instalments is:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{F}= \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{1} & + \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{2}+ \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{3}+\ldots .  \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{t}=\frac{ \mathbf{P}(1+i)\left[(1+i)^{t}-1\right]}{i} \\
 \mathrm{P} & =\text { the amount saved at the start of each month/year } \\
t & =\text { the number of payments (months/years) } \\
i & =\text { the interest rate, expressed as a decimal }
\end{aligned}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-154.jpg?height=588&width=1085&top_left_y=1427&top_left_x=377)

For example, if I save $ 25$ every year for 3 years, the accumulated total (final value) of my savings would be $ 89.13$ at the end of the third year.

## Example 1

Catrona saves $ 400$ every three months for five years at an effective quarterly rate of $0.9 \%$.
(i) Represent her savings by a geometric series
(ii) Find the value of her investment at the end of the period.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& i=0.9 \%=0.009 \\
& 5 \text { years }=(5 \times 4) \text { quarters }=20 \text { payments }
\end{aligned}
$$

(i) Catrona's savings are represented by

$$
400(1.009)+400(1.009)^{2}+400(1.009)^{3}+\ldots+400(1.009)^{20}
$$

(ii) $\left.\begin{array}{rl}a & =400(1.009) \\ r & =(1+i)=1.009 \\ n & =20\end{array}\right\} \begin{aligned} & S_{n}=\frac{a\left(1-r^{n}\right)}{(1-r)} \\ & S_{n}=\frac{400(1.009)\left[1-(1.009)^{20}\right]}{1-1.009}\end{aligned}$
= 8800.89

Note: In all calculations, the rate " $i$ " is converted from a percentage to a decimal.
E.g. $i=5 \%=0.05$.

## Example 2

Find the sum of money, $ \mathrm{P}$, that needs to be saved per month to cover the cost of a $ 1500$ holiday in 18 months time. The interest rate on offer is $0.4 \%$ per month.

The final value (FV) needed $= 1500$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \quad \mathrm{F}= \mathrm{P}(1+i)+ \mathrm{P}(1+i)^{2} \ldots \ldots=\frac{ \mathrm{P}(1+i)\left[(1+i)^{n}-1\right]}{i} \\
& \left.\begin{array}{rl}
i= & 0.4 \% \\
n= & 18
\end{array}\right\} 1+i=1.004 \\
& \therefore \quad \mathrm{FV}= 1500= \mathrm{P}(1.004)+ \mathrm{P}(1.004)^{2} \ldots \ldots  \mathrm{P}(1.004)^{18} \\
& \therefore \quad  1500=\frac{ \mathrm{P}(1.004)\left[(1.004)^{18}-1\right]}{0.004}=\frac{ \mathrm{P} \times 0.0748}{0.004}= \mathrm{P} \times 18.70 \\
& \therefore \quad \mathrm{P}= 1500 \div 18.7 \\
& \quad= 80.21 \text { per month }
\end{aligned}
$$

## 2. Pensions

Similar to instalment savings, we can calculate the sum of money needed to be invested now to guarantee a fixed pension payable over a number of years.
For example, if I want a pension of P per year for the next 20 years, I will need to calculate the present value of each of the payments, $ \mathrm{P}$, I will receive.
The total of these "present value" amounts is the sum of money that needs to be invested.
The sum of $ \mathrm{P}$, paid to you at the end of the first year, has a present value now of $\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)}$.
The sum of $ \mathrm{P}$, paid at the end of the second year, has a present value now of $\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{2}}$.
The sum to be invested is obtained by summing all these amounts.

$$
\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)}+\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{2}}+\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{3}}+\ldots+\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{20}}
$$

This is the sum of a geometric series containing 20 terms, where

$$
\therefore \quad S_{n}=\frac{a\left(1-r^{n}\right)}{(1-r)}=\frac{\mathrm{P}}{(1+i)} \times \frac{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1+i}\right)^{20}\right)}{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1+i}\right)\right)}
$$

$$
\left\{\begin{aligned}
a & =\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)} \\
r & =\frac{1}{(1+i)} \\
n & =20
\end{aligned}\right.
$$

In general, the Present value (cost), $\mathbf{P V}$, of a pension fund is given by:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\mathrm{PV} & =\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)}+\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{2}}+\frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{3}}+\ldots \ldots \frac{ \mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{t}}=\frac{\mathrm{P}}{(1+i)} \times \frac{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1+i}\right)^{t}\right)}{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1+i}\right)\right)} \\
 \mathrm{P} & =\text { yearly pension } \\
n & =\text { number of years (lifetime of pension) } \\
i & =\text { fixed rate of interest over the term } \\
& \text { of the pension }
\end{aligned} \quad=\frac{\mathrm{P}}{(1+i)^{t}}\left(\frac{(1+i)^{t}-1}{i}\right){ }^{i} \text { ) }
$$

Note: Most pensions operate on a guaranteed rate of interest.
However, some may depend on market performance and gain or lose compared to the fixed (guaranteed) rate.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-156.jpg?height=428&width=1024&top_left_y=1743&top_left_x=407)

That is, if I want a pension of  25 every year for 3 years, I need to invest  63.82 now.

## Example 3

What amount of money is needed now to provide a pension of $ 25000$ a year for 20 years, assuming an AER of $4 \%$ ?

$$
i=4 \%=0.04 \Rightarrow 1+i=1.04
$$

The amount required, or present value $(\mathrm{P})$ of this pension, is represented by the geometric series:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \left.\begin{array}{rl}
\mathrm{P}=\frac{25000}{1.04}+\frac{25000}{(1.04)^{2}}+\frac{25000}{(1.04)^{3}}+\ldots \ldots \frac{25000}{(1.04)^{20}} \\
\begin{array}{rl}
a=\frac{ 25000}{(1.04)} \\
n=20
\end{array} \\
r=\frac{1}{1+i}=\frac{1}{1.04}
\end{array}\right\} \begin{aligned}
S_{n}=\frac{a\left(1-r^{n}\right)}{(1-r)} & =\frac{25000}{(1.04)} \times \frac{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1.04}\right)^{20}\right)}{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1.04}\right)\right)} \\
& = 339758.16
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus, a pension fund of $ 339758.16$ invested now will provide 20 yearly payments of 25 000 .

Note 1: In answering questions on instalment savings and pensions it is best practice to set up the geometric series underpinning each question.
It is important to identify the first term, the common ratio and the number of terms in the series for each particular question.

Note 2: It is also important to have a clear understanding of the time-line of series i.e when the first/last payment is to be made/received.

## Example 4

Calculate the final value of an instalment savings plan based on saving 600 at the start of each year @ 4\% per annum for 5 years.
(i) Calculate the present value of these payments.
(ii) Hence show that if the present value was put on deposit at the same rate for the same length of time, it would have the same final value.

Savings plan: $F V=600(1.04)+600(1.04)^{2}+600(1.04)^{3}+600(1.04)^{4}+600(1.04)^{5}$

$$
= 600(1.04)\left(\frac{(1.04)^{5}-1}{0.04}\right)= 3379.79 \text { (at the end of } 5 \text { years) }
$$

(i) Present value $(P V)= 600+\frac{ 600}{(1.04)^{1}}+\frac{ 600}{(1.04)^{2}}+\frac{ 600}{(1.04)^{3}}+\frac{ 600}{(1.04)^{4}}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& = 600 \times \frac{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1.04}\right)^{5}\right)}{\left(1-\left(\frac{1}{1.04}\right)\right)}=\frac{600}{(1.04)^{4}}\left(\frac{(1.04)^{5}-1}{0.04}\right) \\
& = 2777.94 \text { (at the start of the } 5 \text { years) }
\end{aligned}
$$

(ii) If 2777.94 is invested @ 4\% for 5 years its final value is $ 2777.94(1.04)^{5}= 3379.79$ (at the end of the 5 th year).

ICT: Using a graphics calculator or computer software, a comparison between instalment savings and the final value (amount) of a sum of money left on deposit can easily be made.

Saving 600 per year @ 4\%:

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \operatorname{FV}(y)=600^{*}(1.04)\left(1.04^{x}-1\right) /(0.04) \\
& \operatorname{FV}(y)=2778(1.04)^{x} \quad y \text { : the FV in euro } \\
& x \text { : the number of years }
\end{aligned}
$$

2778 on deposit @ 4\%:
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-158.jpg?height=628&width=1196&top_left_y=1096&top_left_x=326)

## Section 5.4 Loans - Mortgages

If we wish to calculate the repayments needed for a car loan or a mortgage on a house, we use the same procedure for finding the present value as was used in the previous section.

The sum of the present values of each repayment over the given period of time must be equal to the value of the car loan or mortgage.
(formulae and tables, p.31)

## Example 1

Calculate the size of the monthly repayments needed for a car loan of $ 10000$ if the loan is to be repaid over a 5 -year term at an effective monthly rate of $0.72 \%$.

$$
\begin{aligned}
& i=0.72 \%=0.0072 \Rightarrow 1+i=1.0072 \\
& n=5 \times 12=60 \text { repayments } \\
& \begin{aligned}
\therefore \quad  \mathrm{~A}(\text { Repayment }) & = P(\text { Loan }) \frac{(i)(1+i)^{t}}{(1+i)^{t}-1}=\frac{ 10000(0.0072)(1.0072)^{60}}{(1.0072)^{60}-1} \\
& \quad= 206 \ldots \text { correct to the nearest euro }
\end{aligned}
\end{aligned}
$$

Note: When using the mortgage/loan formula, each (re)payment is paid at the end of each accounting period, i.e. at the end of a month or at the end of a year.

## Example 2

Find the monthly repayments required for a mortgage of $ 150000$, based on an annual rate of $4.5 \%$ over 20 years.

We first find the effective monthly rate:
$(1+r)^{12}=(1+i)$, where $r \%=$ rate per month and $i \%=$ rate per year.
$\Rightarrow(1+r)^{12}=1.045$
$\Rightarrow(1+r)=(1.045)^{\frac{1}{12}}=1.00367$
$\Rightarrow r=0.00367$ per month $=0.367 \%$ per month
$n=20 \times 12=240$ payments
$\therefore  \mathrm{~A}= P \frac{(i)(1+i)^{t}}{(1+i)^{t}-1}=\frac{ 150000(0.00367)(1.00367)^{240}}{(1.00367)^{240}-1}= 941.22$
Therefore the monthly repayment is $ 941.22$.

## Section 6.1 Revision

In the following table, we revise key properties of 2-dimensional shapes already encountered in your study of mathematics.

| Shape | Diagram | Properties |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| Square | ![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-167.jpg?height=245&width=217&top_left_y=1123&top_left_x=534) | > all sides have the same length <br> $>$ all angles are $90^{\circ}$ <br> > perimeter $=4 x$ <br> > area $=x^{2}$ <br> > diagonal $=\sqrt{2} x$ <br> > diagonals perpendicularly bisect each other |
| Rectangle | ![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-167.jpg?height=200&width=291&top_left_y=1521&top_left_x=499) | > opposite sides have the same length <br> > all angles are $90^{\circ}$ <br> > perimeter $=2(x+y)$ <br> > area $=x y$ <br> > diagonal $=\sqrt{x^{2}+y^{2}}$ <br> > diagonals have the same length <br> > diagonals bisect each other |
| Parallelogram | ![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-167.jpg?height=255&width=327&top_left_y=1845&top_left_x=479) | > opposite sides have the same length <br> > opposite angles are equal <br> > perimeter $=2(x+y)$ <br> > area $=y h=y x \sin \theta$ <br> > diagonals bisect each other |


| Triangle | ![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-168.jpg?height=312&width=314&top_left_y=313&top_left_x=488) | > perimeter $=x+y+z$ <br> > area $=\frac{1}{2}$ x.h $=\frac{1}{2}$ x.z. $\sin \theta$ <br> > $\frac{y}{\sin \theta}=\frac{z}{\sin \alpha}$ <br> > types include isosceles, equilateral, scalene, right-angled <br> > $\alpha+\beta+\theta=180^{\circ}$ <br> > special right-angled triangles with sides <br> - $3,4,5$...... $\left(36.9^{\circ}, 53.1^{\circ}, 90^{\circ}\right)$ <br> - $1, \sqrt{3}, 2, \ldots . . .\left(30^{\circ}, 60^{\circ}, 90^{\circ}\right)$ <br> - $1,1, \sqrt{2} \ldots \ldots .\left(45^{\circ}, 45^{\circ}, 90^{\circ}\right)$ |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |

## 1. Trapezium

A trapezium is a quadrilateral which has one pair of parallel sides.
The area of a trapezium $=a h_{\text {parallelogram }}+\frac{1}{2}(b-a) h_{\text {triangle }}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
= & a h+\frac{1}{2} b h-\frac{1}{2} a h \\
= & \frac{1}{2} a h+\frac{1}{2} b h=\left(\frac{a+b}{2}\right) h \\
= & \text { half the sum of the lengths of the } \\
& \text { parallel sides times the height. }
\end{aligned}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-168.jpg?height=341&width=456&top_left_y=862&top_left_x=1177)

## Example 1

If a parallelogram has a base of 10 cm , and a trapezium of the same area and height has a base of 14 cm , find $x$, the length of the other parallel side of the trapezium.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-168.jpg?height=225&width=661&top_left_y=1472&top_left_x=869)

Area of parallelogram $=$ Base $\times$ perpendicular height $(h)=10 h$
Area of trapezium $=\frac{1}{2}(x+14) \times$ perpendicular height $(h)=\frac{1}{2}(x+14) h$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\therefore & & 10 h & =\frac{1}{2}(x+14) h \\
\Rightarrow & & 20 & =x+14 \\
\Rightarrow & x & =6 \quad \Rightarrow & \text { the other parallel side }=6 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}
$$

## POLYGONS

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-169.jpg?height=258&width=293&top_left_y=262&top_left_x=926)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-169.jpg?height=314&width=324&top_left_y=206&top_left_x=1234)
heptagon

## 2. Polygons

A polygon is a plane (2-dimensional) shape with straight edges.
Regular polygons are symmetrical, with a base triangle repeated in polygons with more than 4 sides. The interior angles of regular polygons are:
Triangle $=60^{\circ}$, Quadrilateral $=90^{\circ}$, Pentagon $=108^{\circ}$, Hexagon $=120^{\circ}$, Heptagon $=128.6^{\circ}$

## Example 2

The area of the regular pentagon shown here is $600 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$.
Calculate the length of one side, $x$, of the pentagon.
Since the total angle at the centre of the pentagon $=360^{\circ}$,
$\therefore \quad$ each angle at the centre $=\frac{360^{\circ}}{5}=72^{\circ}$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-169.jpg?height=267&width=303&top_left_y=1064&top_left_x=1230)

Each triangle is congruent and has equal base angles $=\frac{(180-72)^{\circ}}{2}=54^{\circ}$
Each triangle has an area of $\left(\frac{600}{5}\right) \mathrm{cm}^{2}=120 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$.
$\operatorname{Tan} 54^{\circ}=\frac{h}{\frac{x}{2}} \Rightarrow h=\frac{x}{2} \tan 54^{\circ}$
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-169.jpg?height=258&width=301&top_left_y=1493&top_left_x=1231)

The area of the triangle $=\frac{1}{2} \times$ base $\times h=\frac{1}{2} \times x \times h=120 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$

$$
=\frac{1}{2} \times x \times \frac{x}{2} \tan 54^{\circ}=120 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}
$$

$\Rightarrow \quad x^{2}=\frac{480}{\tan 54^{\circ}}=348.74 \Rightarrow x=18.675=18.7 \mathrm{~cm}$

(Note; arrows indicate parallel lines.)

5. A rectangular wooden frame is built to lay a concrete foundation for a patio.

To support the frame while the concrete is being poured, steel cables are fixed diagonally across the rectangle and protrude out of the frame by 50 cm .

7. E is the midpoint of [DC].

Draw the image of the trapezium ABCD rotated by $180^{\circ}$ about the point E .
(i) What shape is made by the image and the original trapezium?
(ii) What is the area of this composite shape?
(iii) Explain how this proves the formula for
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-170.jpg?height=308&width=411&top_left_y=1659&top_left_x=1218) the area of a trapezium.
8. Three times the width of a certain rectangle exceeds twice its length by 3 cm , and four times its length is 12 cm more than its perimeter. Find the dimensions of the rectangle.

He wants to make a trapezium shape with two right angles as shown. The dotted line completes the trapezium.
Draw the three possible arrangements of rods.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-171.jpg?height=242&width=435&top_left_y=169&top_left_x=1197)

Given the inequality above, find which arrangement has the greatest area.
(Note: if $a>b$, then $a c>b c$, given $c>0$ )

(Diagram not to scale.)

(ii) The length of one side of an equilateral triangle is 10.75 cm .

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-171.jpg?height=310&width=489&top_left_y=1137&top_left_x=1142)

## Section 6.2 Sectors of circles

1. Revision of circles and sectors of circles

| Circle/ Disc | ![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-172.jpg?height=319&width=325&top_left_y=1460&top_left_x=494) | - perimeter $($ circumference $)=\ell$; since $\frac{\ell}{2 r}=\pi$ $\Rightarrow \ell=2 \pi r$ <br> - area $=\pi r^{2}$ <br> - a cyclic quadrilateral is a quadrilateral inscribed in a circle <br> - every triangle inscribed in a semicircle is a right-angled triangle <br> - $\mathbf{3 6 0}{ }^{\circ}=\mathbf{2} \boldsymbol{\pi}$ radians |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |

2. Arc of a circle

In the chapter on trigonometry, the length of an arc, the area of a sector, and radian measure were introduced. The length of an arc of a circle is found using the ratios

$$
\frac{\ell}{2 \pi r}=\frac{\theta(\text { degrees })}{360}=\frac{\theta(\text { radians })}{2 \pi}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-172.jpg?height=390&width=529&top_left_y=1870&top_left_x=1105)

$$
\begin{aligned}
\therefore \text { Length of } \operatorname{arc}(l)=2 \pi r \frac{\theta(\text { degrees })}{360} & =2 \pi r \frac{\theta(\text { radians })}{2 \pi} \\
= & r \theta(\theta \text { in radians })
\end{aligned}
$$

## 3. Area of a sector

Similarly, the area of a sector of a circle is found using the ratios

$$
\frac{A}{\pi r^{2}}=\frac{\theta(\text { degrees })}{360}=\frac{\theta(\text { radians })}{2 \pi}
$$

$\therefore$ Area of sector $(A)=\pi r^{2} \frac{\theta(\text { degrees })}{360}=\pi r^{2} \frac{\theta(\text { radians })}{2 \pi}=\frac{1}{2} r^{2} \theta(\theta$ in radians $)$

## Example 1

A flowerbed in the shape of a section of a sector of a circle is placed in the centre of a rectangular lawn, as shown in the diagram. Calculate
(i) the length of edging needed for the flowerbed
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-173.jpg?height=272&width=522&top_left_y=1071&top_left_x=1012)
(ii) the area of grass in the garden.

Correct each answer to one place of decimals.
(i) length of large arc $\mathrm{IH}=2 \pi r \frac{\theta(\text { degrees })}{360}=\left(\frac{90}{360} 2 \pi 8\right) \mathrm{m}=4 \pi \mathrm{~m}$

$$
\begin{array}{l|r}
\text { length of small arc JK }=\left(\frac{90}{360} 2 \pi 3\right) \mathrm{m} & \begin{array}{r}
\text { Total perimeter }= \\
4 \pi+\frac{3 \pi}{2}+(2 \times 5)=\left(\frac{11 \pi}{2}+10\right) \mathrm{m} \\
=\frac{3 \pi}{2} \mathrm{~m}
\end{array}
\end{array}
$$

(ii) The area of the sector (flowerbed portion) $=$

Area of large sector - Area of small sector

$$
=\left(\frac{90}{360}\right) \pi 8^{2} \mathrm{~m}^{2}-\left(\frac{90}{360}\right) \pi 3^{2} \mathrm{~m}^{2}=\left(\frac{90}{360}\right) 55 \pi \mathrm{~m}^{2}=43.197 \mathrm{~m}^{2}
$$

Area of rectangle $=(8 \times 16) \mathrm{m}^{2}=128 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$
$\therefore \quad$ The area of grass $=(128-43.197) \mathrm{m}^{2}=84.8 \mathrm{~m}^{2}$

## Example 2

A minor arc CD of a circle, centre O and radius 20 cm , subtends an angle $x$ radians at O . The major arc CD of the circle subtends an angle $5 x$ radians at O .
Find, in terms of $\pi$, the length of the minor arc.

$$
\begin{aligned}
x+5 x & =2 \pi \\
x & =\frac{2 \pi}{6}=\frac{\pi}{3}
\end{aligned}
$$

The length of the minor $\operatorname{arc}=r \theta=20 x=\frac{20 \pi}{3} \mathrm{~cm}$
(Note: This is an exact measurement. An approximate answer
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_07_30_8707c24399a8bd6d62d8g-174.jpg?height=312&width=298&top_left_y=379&top_left_x=1254) is obtained when we substitute a value for $\pi$.)
